Sure, I can provide you with some websites that offer English sentences with native speaker pronunciation along with their pros and cons.

1. Forvo (https://forvo.com/)
Pros:
- Provides pronunciations by native speakers from all around the world
- Offers a wide range of words and phrases
- Allows users to request pronunciations

Cons:
- Limited to individual words and short phrases
- Some pronunciations may not be of the highest quality

2. Pronunciation Studio (https://pronunciationstudio.com/)
Pros:
- Provides detailed information on English pronunciation, including sounds, stress, and intonation
- Offers a range of courses and resources for learners of all levels
- Features native speakers with clear and concise pronunciation

Cons:
- Some of the resources require payment
- May be more geared towards learners who want to focus on British English

3. YouGlish (https://youglish.com/)
Pros:
- Provides pronunciations from native speakers in a variety of English-speaking countries
- Allows users to search for specific words in context, which can help with understanding pronunciation in real-world situations
- Offers a range of features, including slow motion and looping of videos

Cons:
- Some pronunciations may be less clear or less accurate than others
- Requires some effort to search for specific words or phrases

4. Speechling (https://speechling.com/)
Pros:
- Offers a wide range of resources for learners of all levels, including pronunciation practice, grammar, and vocabulary
- Provides feedback on pronunciation and allows users to track their progress
- Features native speakers with clear and natural-sounding pronunciation

Cons:
- Some of the resources require payment
- May be more geared towards learners who want to focus on American English

Overall, these websites can be helpful resources for learners who want to improve their English pronunciation. However, it's important to keep in mind that pronunciation can vary depending on factors such as regional accents and context, so it's a good idea to practice with a variety of sources and seek feedback from a teacher or tutor if possible.

Sure, here are some additional websites that offer English sentences with native speaker pronunciation along with their pros and cons:

5. ELSA Speak (https://elsaspeak.com/)
Pros:
- Uses artificial intelligence to provide personalized feedback on pronunciation
- Offers a range of exercises and games to practice pronunciation
- Features native speakers with clear and natural-sounding pronunciation

Cons:
- Some of the resources require payment
- May be more geared towards learners who want to focus on American English

6. Speak Good English Movement (https://www.goodenglish.org.sg/)
Pros:
- Provides a range of resources for learners of all levels, including pronunciation practice, grammar, and vocabulary
- Offers a variety of video and audio resources with native speaker pronunciation
- Geared towards learners who want to improve their English in a Singaporean context

Cons:
- May be less relevant for learners who want to focus on other English-speaking countries or regions
- Some of the resources may be more oriented towards Singaporean English and its unique features.

7. English Central (https://www.englishcentral.com/)
Pros:
- Offers a range of video resources with native speaker pronunciation, including news and entertainment clips
- Provides personalized feedback on pronunciation and allows users to track their progress
- Geared towards learners who want to improve their listening and speaking skills through real-world context

Cons:
- Some of the resources require payment
- May be less effective for learners who want to focus on pronunciation in isolation rather than in context

Overall, these websites can also be helpful resources for learners who want to improve their English pronunciation and speaking skills. It's important to consider your goals and needs as a learner when choosing which resources to use, and to supplement your practice with additional resources and feedback as needed.

  "text": "\n한국의 새해 기념일(설날)은 대개 3일 동안 진행됩니다: 이는 한국의 설날 전 후로 하여 열리게 됩니다.  \n\n2월 10 토요일 【2†한국의 설날†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n한국의 설날은 음력 첫 날에 열리며 가장 크고 중요한 명절 중 하나입니다.  \n\n2월 12 월요일 【2†한국의 설날†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n. 명절의 셋째날 – 한국의 설날 다음 날.  \n\n2월 14 수요일 【3†발렌타인 데이†ko.wikipedia.org】  대체휴무일 \n\n발렌타인 데이는 문화적 상징이자 종교적 및 상업적인 행사로서 로맨스 및 로맨틱한 사랑의 기념하는 날입니다.  \n\n3월 3월 1 금요일 독립 운동 기념일  공휴일 \n\n1910년부터 1945년가지 일본의 한국 통치 기간 동안 한국에서 진행된 다양한 독립 운동을 기리는 날.  \n\n4 월 5 금요일 【4†식목일†ko.wikipedia.org】  대체휴무일 \n\n나무를 심는 행사를 하는 날입니다. 한국에서는 4월 5일에 진행됩니다.  \n\n5월 1 수요일 【5†노동절†ko.wikipedia.org】  대체휴무일 \n\n유럽에서 매년 5월 1일은 봄 축제이자 노동자를 기리는 근로자의 날 기념 행사가 이루어집니다.  \n\n5월 5 일요일 【6†어린이날†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n이는 어린이들을 위하고 축하하는 날로 한국과 일본에서는 5월 5일에 이뤄집니다.  \n\n5월 8 수요일 【7†어버이날†ko.wikipedia.org】  대체휴무일 \n\n어버이날은 자녀가 부모에게 고마움과 사랑을 표하는 날입니다.  \n\n5월 15 수요일 【8†석가탄신일†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n본 기념일은 전통적으로 동아시아 대부분의 지역에서 고타마 싯다르타 왕자의 생일을 기념하기 위해 행해지는 기념일입니다. 이는 음력으로 4번째 달의 8일째 날에 이루어집니다.  \n\n5월 15 수요일 스승의 날  대체휴무일 \n\n스승에게 감사와 경의를 표하는 날입니다.  \n\n6월 6 목요일 【9†현충일†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n한국 전쟁 및 국가 내에서 진행된 전쟁 및 전투에 의해 전사한 군인을 포함한 모든 희생자들을 기리는 날입니다.  \n\n7월 17 수요일 【10†제헌절†ko.wikipedia.org】  대체휴무일 \n\n1948년에 대한민국에서 최초로 헌법이 공포된 것을 기념하기 위해 제정된 날입니다.  \n\n8월 15 목요일 【11†광복절†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n이는 미국과 소련이 1910년부터 1945년까지 지속된 일본의 통치로부터 한국으로 해방시킨 날로서 자유 및 승리를 기념하기 위해 제정된 날입니다.  \n\n9월 16 월요일 【12†추석 명절†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n이는 말 그대로 “가을 전야제”이며 가장 큰 추수 감사절입니다. 북한과 남한의 음력 8월 15일 보름에 개최되며 약 3일간 이루어집니다.  \n\n9월 17 화요일 【12†추석 명절†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n추석의 두번째 날 – 추석 명절은 한국의 전통적인 명절 중 하나로서 고향으로 돌아가 친지 및 가족을 만나고 함께 성대한 식사를 하는 일종의 추수 감사절입니다.  \n\n9월 18 수요일 【12†추석 명절†ko.wikipedia.org】  공휴일 \n\n추석 세번째 날 – 추수 감사절로서 추분 시기에 개최되는 명절입니다.  \n\n10월 1 화요일 【13†국군의 날†ko.wikipedia.org】  대체휴무일 \n\n한국 국군에서 군대에 복무중인 남성 및 여성 모두에게 있어 공로를 기념하기 위해 ",

인반도와 아웃반도는 기본적으로 "인바운드"와 "아웃바운드" 마케팅 전략을 의미합니다. 인바운드 마케팅은 고객이 자발적으로 찾아오도록 유도하는 전략으로, 블로그, 소셜 미디어, 검색 엔진 최적화(SEO) 등을 활용합니다. 반면, 아웃바운드 마케팅은 기업이 직접적으로 고객에게 접근하는 방식으로, 광고, 이메일 마케팅, 전화 마케팅 등이 포함됩니다.

인바운드 마케팅의 경우, 콘텐츠를 통해 고객에게 가치를 제공하고 신뢰를 쌓아 자연스럽게 제품이나 서비스에 관심을 갖게 하는 방식입니다. 아웃바운드 마케팅은 주로 단기적인 성과를 목표로 하며, 다양한 채널을 통해 직접적으로 메시지를 전달해 고객의 관심을 끌고 행동을 유도합니다【6†source】【8†source】.

신호등이 평소보다 더 밝게 느껴지는 이유는 여러 가지가 있을 수 있어요. 먼저, 눈의 피로도나 상태가 영향을 미칠 수 있어요. 만약 눈이 피로하거나 건조하면 빛에 더 민감하게 반응할 수 있습니다. 또한, 날씨 조건도 영향을 줄 수 있습니다. 맑은 날이나 어두운 시간대에 신호등이 더 밝게 보일 수 있어요.

또 다른 가능성은 신호등 자체의 문제일 수 있습니다. 예를 들어, 신호등의 조명이 너무 강하거나 LED 전구가 교체된 경우, 더 밝게 느껴질 수 있습니다【6†source】【8†source】.

이러한 현상이 계속된다면, 안과 검진을 받아보는 것도 좋은 방법이에요. 시력에 변화가 생기거나 눈의 건강에 문제가 있을 수 있기 때문이에요.

### Message 15 — assistant

- Timestamp: 2024-05-24T12:28:58.891609+00:00
- Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "25d3611d-a4e0-4457-901d-c4d1f15e629b", "parent_id": "cfdaede2-e4ac-4820-90df-f92b2882b5ba", "create_time": "2024-05-24T12:28:58.891609+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"citations\":[],\"gizmo_id\":null,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4o\",\"default_model_slug\":\"auto\",\"voice_mode_message\":true,\"pad\":\"AAAAA\",\"parent_id\":\"cfdaede2-e4ac-4820-90df-f92b2882b5ba\",\"finish_details\":{\"type\":\"stop\",\"stop_tokens\":[200002]},\"is_complete\":true,\"request_id\":\"d4be8d41-ecb8-4757-8149-9812556df9ee\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\"}"}`


수학적으로 시간이나 어떤 값을 0으로 나누는 것은 불가능합니다. 이를 수학적으로 "0으로 나눌 수 없다"라고 표현합니다. 이유는 0으로 나누는 순간, 수학적 의미가 없어지고 무한대나 정의되지 않은 상태가 되기 때문입니다. 예를 들어, 1을 0으로 나누면 그 결과는 무한대가 되지만, 이는 실제로 수학적 의미를 가지지 않습니다.

수학적으로 이를 이해하려면 분수를 생각해 볼 수 있습니다. 예를 들어, \(\frac{a}{b}\)에서 b가 0이라면, 이 식은 정의되지 않습니다. 이는 모든 수학 체계에서 기본적인 규칙입니다.

또한, 물리학적으로도 시간이나 다른 물리적 단위를 0으로 나누는 것은 의미가 없습니다. 이는 실제 세계에서 일어나지 않는 현상입니다.

### Message 17 — assistant

- Timestamp: 2024-05-24T12:30:25.726519+00:00

아, "시청해주셔서 감사합니다"라는 말은 유튜브나 방송에서 자주 쓰이는 인사말이죠. 이런 상황에서는 시청자들에게 감사를 표하는 말입니다. 도움이 되셨다면 기쁘네요! 혹시 더 알고 싶은 것이 있으신가요?

상태를 "빠른 상태"와 "느린 상태"로만 나눈다고 하면 이는 매우 단순화된 관점입니다. 실제로 물리학, 생물학, 심리학 등 다양한 분야에서는 상태를 훨씬 더 복잡하게 분류합니다.

예를 들어, 물리학에서는 물질의 상태를 고체, 액체, 기체, 플라즈마 등으로 나눕니다. 생물학에서는 생물의 활동 상태를 휴면, 활동, 성장 등으로 구분할 수 있습니다. 심리학에서는 감정 상태, 인지 상태 등 다양한 상태를 다룹니다.

빠른 상태와 느린 상태는 단순화된 이분법으로, 특정 상황에서 유용할 수 있지만, 대부분의 경우 더 많은 상태와 조건들이 존재합니다. 실제 상황에서는 다양한 변수와 상태를 고려하는 것이 필요합니다【6†source】【8†source】.

### Message 13 — tool

- Timestamp: 2023-12-27T01:15:58.727997+00:00
- Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "285b4c0b-043c-4e3f-a976-6e31d79d034f", "parent_id": "f2b656e5-69c9-4062-a68f-e58f583f8753", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:15:58.727997+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"_cite_metadata\":{\"citation_format\":{\"name\":\"tether_og\"},\"metadata_list\":[{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Which-way detector unlocks some mystery of the double-slit experiment\",\"url\":\"https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html\",\"text\":\"\\nThroughout the years, scientists have demonstrated different versions of the two-slit experiment. In the new study, physicists Stefano Frabboni from the University of Modena and Reggio Emilia and the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience in Modena, Italy; Gian Carlo Gazzadi from the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience; and Giulio Pozzi from the University of Bologna have presented another version of the two-slit experiment using a transmission electron microscope.\\n\\n“Over the last few years, we tried to use our expertise in transmission electron microscopy and focused ion beam specimen preparation to realize some basic experiments related to some of the ‘mysteries’ of quantum mechanics, as pointed out by Feynman in his celebrated lectures and books,” Frabboni told PhysOrg.com.\\n\\nFirst, the scientists used focused ion beam milling to make two nanoslits on a barrier. Then they modified one of the slits by covering it with a filter made of several layers of “low atomic number” material to create a which-way detector for the electrons passing through.\\n\\nAlthough the electrons (which were shot one by one) could still pass through the filtered slit, the filter caused more of the electrons to undergo inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering. As the physicists explained, an electron undergoing inelastic scattering is localized at the covered slit, and acts like a spherical wave after passing through the slit. In contrast, an electron passing through the unfiltered slit is more likely to undergo elastic scattering, and act like a cylindrical wave after passing through that slit. The spherical wave and cylindrical wave do not have any phase correlation, and so even if an electron passed through both slits, the two different waves that come out cannot create an interference pattern on the wall behind them.\\n\\nThe physicists also found that the thickness of the filter determined the interference effects: the thicker the filter, the greater the probability for inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering, and so the fewer the interference effects. They could make the filter thick enough so that the interference effects canceled out almost completely.\\n\\n“When the electron suffers inelastic scattering, it is localized; this means that its wavefunction collapses and after the measurement act, it propagates roughly as a spherical wave from the region of interaction, with no phase relation at all with other elastically or inelastically scattered electrons,” Frabboni said. “The experimental results show electrons through two slits (so two bright lines in the image when elastic and inelastic scattered electrons are collected) with negligible interference effects in the one-slit Fraunhofer diffraction pattern formed with elastic electrons.”\\n\\nIn a separate study, the physicists covered both slits to see if two spherical waves would create an interference pattern. They found that, in the very faint inelastic intensity, no fringes seem present, whereas interference fringes are recovered, at a very low intensity, when the elastic image is taken.\\n\\nOverall, the results suggest that the type of scattering an electron undergoes determines the mark it leaves on the back wall, and that a detector at one of the slits can change the type of scattering. The physicists concluded that, while elastically scattered electrons can cause an interference pattern, the inelastically scattered electrons do not contribute to the interference process.\\n\\nMore information: Stefano Frabboni, Gian Carlo Gazzadi, and Giulio Pozzi. “Ion and electron beam nanofabrication of the which-way double-slit experiment in a transmission electron microscope.” Applied Physics Letters 97, 263101 (2010). 【26†DOI:10.1063/1.3529947†dx.doi.org】 \\n\\nCopyright 2010 PhysOrg.com.  \\nAll rights reserved. {
  "content_type": "tether_quote",
  "url": "https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html",
  "domain": "phys.org",
  "text": "\nThroughout the years, scientists have demonstrated different versions of the two-slit experiment. In the new study, physicists Stefano Frabboni from the University of Modena and Reggio Emilia and the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience in Modena, Italy; Gian Carlo Gazzadi from the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience; and Giulio Pozzi from the University of Bologna have presented another version of the two-slit experiment using a transmission electron microscope.\n\n“Over the last few years, we tried to use our expertise in transmission electron microscopy and focused ion beam specimen preparation to realize some basic experiments related to some of the ‘mysteries’ of quantum mechanics, as pointed out by Feynman in his celebrated lectures and books,” Frabboni told PhysOrg.com.\n\nFirst, the scientists used focused ion beam milling to make two nanoslits on a barrier. Then they modified one of the slits by covering it with a filter made of several layers of “low atomic number” material to create a which-way detector for the electrons passing through.\n\nAlthough the electrons (which were shot one by one) could still pass through the filtered slit, the filter caused more of the electrons to undergo inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering. As the physicists explained, an electron undergoing inelastic scattering is localized at the covered slit, and acts like a spherical wave after passing through the slit. In contrast, an electron passing through the unfiltered slit is more likely to undergo elastic scattering, and act like a cylindrical wave after passing through that slit. The spherical wave and cylindrical wave do not have any phase correlation, and so even if an electron passed through both slits, the two different waves that come out cannot create an interference pattern on the wall behind them.\n\nThe physicists also found that the thickness of the filter determined the interference effects: the thicker the filter, the greater the probability for inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering, and so the fewer the interference effects. They could make the filter thick enough so that the interference effects canceled out almost completely.\n\n“When the electron suffers inelastic scattering, it is localized; this means that its wavefunction collapses and after the measurement act, it propagates roughly as a spherical wave from the region of interaction, with no phase relation at all with other elastically or inelastically scattered electrons,” Frabboni said. “The experimental results show electrons through two slits (so two bright lines in the image when elastic and inelastic scattered electrons are collected) with negligible interference effects in the one-slit Fraunhofer diffraction pattern formed with elastic electrons.”\n\nIn a separate study, the physicists covered both slits to see if two spherical waves would create an interference pattern. They found that, in the very faint inelastic intensity, no fringes seem present, whereas interference fringes are recovered, at a very low intensity, when the elastic image is taken.\n\nOverall, the results suggest that the type of scattering an electron undergoes determines the mark it leaves on the back wall, and that a detector at one of the slits can change the type of scattering. The physicists concluded that, while elastically scattered electrons can cause an interference pattern, the inelastically scattered electrons do not contribute to the interference process.\n\nMore information: Stefano Frabboni, Gian Carlo Gazzadi, and Giulio Pozzi. “Ion and electron beam nanofabrication of the which-way double-slit experiment in a transmission electron microscope.” Applied Physics Letters 97, 263101 (2010). 【26†DOI:10.1063/1.3529947†dx.doi.org】 \n\nCopyright 2010 PhysOrg.com.  \nAll rights reserved. This material may not be published, broadcast, rewritten or redistributed in whole or part without the express written permission of PhysOrg.com. \n\nCitation: Which-way detector unlocks some mystery of the double-slit experiment (2011, January 21) retrieved 26 December 2023 from https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html \n\nThis document is subject to copyright. Apart from any fair dealing for the purpose of private study or research, no part may be reproduced without the written permission. The idea of using particles other than photons in the experiment did not come until after the ideas of de Broglie and the advent of quantum mechanics, when it was proposed that fundamental particles might also behave as waves with characteristic wavelengths depending on their momenta. The single-electron version of the experiment was in fact not performed until 1974. A more recent version of the experiment successfully demonstrating wave-particle duality used 【20†buckminsterfullerene】 or buckyballs, the \\\\(C_{60}\\\\) 【21†allotrope】 of carbon.\\n\\n#### Contents\\n\\n  * 【22†Waves vs. Particles】 \\n  * 【23†Double-slit Experiment with Electrons】 \\n  * 【24†Modeling the Double-slit Experiment】 \\n  * 【25†References】 \\n\\n## Waves vs. Particles\\n\\nTo understand why the double-slit experiment is important, it is useful to understand the strong distinctions between wave and particles that make wave-particle duality so intriguing.\\n\\nWaves describe oscillating values of a physical quantity that obey the 【26†wave equation】. They are usually described by sums of 【27†sine and cosine】 functions, since any periodic (oscillating) function may be decomposed into a 【28†Fourier series】. When two waves pass through each other, the resulting wave is the sum of the two original waves. This is called a superposition since the waves are placed (\\\"-position\\\") on top of each other (\\\"super-\\\"). Superposition is one of the most fundamental principles of quantum mechanics. A general quantum system need not be in one state or another but can reside in a superposition of two where there is some probability of 【29†measuring】 the quantum 【29†wavefunction】 in one state or another.\\n\\n[Image 1: Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.] Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.【30†[2]】\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 2 \\\\sin (2x)\\\\). The addition of two waves to form a wave of larger amplitude is in general known as constructive interference since the interference results in a larger wave.\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 0\\\\) \\\\(\\\\big(\\\\)since \\\\(\\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi) = - \\\\sin (2x)\\\\big).\\\\) This is known as destructive interference in general, when adding two waves results in a wave of smaller amplitude. See the figure above for examples of both constructive and destructive interference.\\n\\na) only  a) and b)  b) and c)  c) only \\n\\n[Image 2] \\n\\nTwo speakers are generating sounds with the same phase, amplitude, and wavelength. The two sound waves can make constructive interference, as above left. Or they can make destructive interference, as above right. If we want to find out the exact position where the two sounds make destructive interference, which of the following do we need to know?\\n\\na) the wavelength of the sound waves   \\nb) the distances from the two speakers   \\nc) the speed of sound generated by the two speakers\\n\\nThis wave behavior is quite unlike the behavior of particles. Classically, particles are objects with a single definite position and a single definite momentum. Particles do not make interference patterns with other particles in detectors whether or not they pass through slits. They only interact by 【31†colliding elastically】, i.e., via electromagnetic forces at short distances. Before the discovery of quantum mechanics, it was assumed that waves and particles were two distinct models for objects, and that any real physical thing could only be described as a particle or as a wave, but not both.\\n\\n## Double-slit Experiment with Electrons\\n\\nIn the more modern version of the double slit experiment using electrons, electrons with the same momentum are shot from an \\\"electron gun\\\" like the ones inside CRT televisions towards a screen with two slits in it. After each electron goes through one of the slits, it is observed hitting a single point on a detecting screen at an apparently random location. As more and more electrons pass through, one at a time, they form an overall pattern of light and dark interference bands. If each electron was truly just a point particle, then there would only be two clusters of observations: one for the electrons passing through the left slit, and one for the right. However, if electrons are made of waves, they interfere with themselves and pass through both slits simultaneously. Indeed, this is what is observed when the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons. It must therefore be true that the electron is interfering with itself since each electron was only sent through one at a time—there were no other electrons to interfere with it!\\n\\nWhen the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons instead of photons, the relevant wavelength is the de Broglie wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda:\\\\)\\n\\n\\\\[\\\\lambda = \\\\frac{h}{p},\\\\]\\n\\nwhere \\\\(h\\\\) is Planck's constant and \\\\(p\\\\) is the electron's momentum. \\n\\n\\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\] \\n\\nCalculate the de Broglie wavelength of an electron moving with velocity \\\\(1.0 \\\\times 10^{7} \\\\text{ m/s}.\\\\)\\n\\nSubmit your answer\\n\\nUsain Bolt, the world champion sprinter, hit a top speed of 27.79 miles per hour at the Olympics. If he has a mass of 94 kg, what was his de Broglie wavelength?\\n\\nExpress your answer as an order of magnitude in units of the Bohr radius \\\\(r_{B} = 5.29 \\\\times 10^{-11} \\\\text{m}\\\\). For instance, if your answer was \\\\(4 \\\\times 10^{-5} r_{B}\\\\), your should give \\\\(-5.\\\\)\\n\\n[Image 3] \\n\\n* * *\\n\\n###### Image Credit: Flickr drcliffordchoi.\\n\\nWhile the de Broglie relation was postulated for massive matter, the equation applies equally well to light. Given light of a certain wavelength, the momentum and energy of that light can be found using de Broglie's formula. This generalizes the naive formula \\\\(p = m v\\\\), which can't be applied to light since light has no mass and always moves at a constant velocity of \\\\(c\\\\) regardless of wavelength.\\n\\n## Modeling the Double-slit Experiment\\n\\nThe below is reproduced from the 【32†Amplitude, Frequency, Wave Number, Phase Shift】 wiki.\\n\\nIn Young's double-slit experiment, photons corresponding to light of wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda\\\\) are fired at a barrier with two thin slits separated by a distance \\\\(d,\\\\) as shown in the diagram below. After passing through the slits, they hit a screen at a distance of \\\\(D\\\\) away with \\\\(D \\\\gg d,\\\\) and the point of impact is measured. Remarkably, both the experiment and theory of 【13†quantum mechanics】 predict that the number of photons measured at each point along the screen follows a complicated series of peaks and troughs called an interference pattern as below. The photons must exhibit the wave behavior of a relative phase shift somehow to be responsible for this phenomenon. Below, the condition for which maxima of the interference pattern occur on the screen is derived.\\n\\n[Image 4: Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. ] Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. 【33†[3]】\\n\\nSince \\\\(D \\\\gg d\\\\), the angle from each of the slits is approximately the same and equal to \\\\(\\\\theta\\\\). If \\\\(y\\\\) is the vertical displacement to an interference peak from the midpoint between the slits, it is therefore true that\\n\\n\\\\[D\\\\tan \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\sin \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\theta = y.\\\\]\\n\\nFurthermore, there is a path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) between the two slits and the interference peak. Light from the lower slit must travel \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) further to reach any particular spot on the screen, as in the diagram below:\\n\\n[Image 5: Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.] Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.\\n\\nThe condition for constructive interference is that the path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) is exactly equal to an integer number of wavelengths.   "text": "\nThe original double-slit experiment was performed using light/photons around the turn of the nineteenth century by Thomas Young, so the original experiment is often called Young's double-slit experiment. The idea of using particles other than photons in the experiment did not come until after the ideas of de Broglie and the advent of quantum mechanics, when it was proposed that fundamental particles might also behave as waves with characteristic wavelengths depending on their momenta. The single-electron version of the experiment was in fact not performed until 1974. A more recent version of the experiment successfully demonstrating wave-particle duality used 【20†buckminsterfullerene】 or buckyballs, the \\(C_{60}\\) 【21†allotrope】 of carbon.\n\n#### Contents\n\n  * 【22†Waves vs. Particles】 \n  * 【23†Double-slit Experiment with Electrons】 \n  * 【24†Modeling the Double-slit Experiment】 \n  * 【25†References】 \n\n## Waves vs. Particles\n\nTo understand why the double-slit experiment is important, it is useful to understand the strong distinctions between wave and particles that make wave-particle duality so intriguing.\n\nWaves describe oscillating values of a physical quantity that obey the 【26†wave equation】. They are usually described by sums of 【27†sine and cosine】 functions, since any periodic (oscillating) function may be decomposed into a 【28†Fourier series】. When two waves pass through each other, the resulting wave is the sum of the two original waves. This is called a superposition since the waves are placed (\"-position\") on top of each other (\"super-\"). Superposition is one of the most fundamental principles of quantum mechanics. A general quantum system need not be in one state or another but can reside in a superposition of two where there is some probability of 【29†measuring】 the quantum 【29†wavefunction】 in one state or another.\n\n[Image 1: Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.] Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.【30†[2]】\n\nIf one wave is \\(A(x) = \\sin (2x)\\) and the other is \\(B(x) = \\sin (2x)\\), then they add together to make \\(A + B = 2 \\sin (2x)\\). The addition of two waves to form a wave of larger amplitude is in general known as constructive interference since the interference results in a larger wave.\n\nIf one wave is \\(A(x) = \\sin (2x)\\) and the other is \\(B(x) = \\sin (2x + \\pi)\\), then they add together to make \\(A + B = 0\\) \\(\\big(\\)since \\(\\sin (2x + \\pi) = - \\sin (2x)\\big).\\) This is known as destructive interference in general, when adding two waves results in a wave of smaller amplitude. See the figure above for examples of both constructive and destructive interference.\n\na) only  a) and b)  b) and c)  c) only \n\n[Image 2] \n\nTwo speakers are generating sounds with the same phase, amplitude, and wavelength. The two sound waves can make constructive interference, as above left. Or they can make destructive interference, as above right. If we want to find out the exact position where the two sounds make destructive interference, which of the following do we need to know?\n\na) the wavelength of the sound waves   \nb) the distances from the two speakers   \nc) the speed of sound generated by the two speakers\n\nThis wave behavior is quite unlike the behavior of particles. Classically, particles are objects with a single definite position and a single definite momentum. Particles do not make interference patterns with other particles in detectors whether or not they pass through slits. They only interact by 【31†colliding elastically】, i.e., via electromagnetic forces at short distances. Before the discovery of quantum mechanics, it was assumed that waves and particles were two distinct models for objects, and that any real physical thing could only be described as a particle or as a wave, but not both.\n\n## Double-slit Experiment with Electrons\n\nIn the more modern version of the double slit experiment using electrons, electrons with the same momentum are shot from an \"electron gun\" like the ones inside CRT televisions towards a screen with two slits in it. After each electron goes through one of the slits, it is observed hitting a single point on a detecting screen at an apparently random location. As more and more electrons pass through, one at a time, they form an overall pattern of light and dark interference bands. If each electron was truly just a point particle, then there would only be two clusters of observations: one for the electrons passing through the left slit, and one for the right. However, if electrons are made of waves, they interfere with themselves and pass through both slits simultaneously. Indeed, this is what is observed when the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons. It must therefore be true that the electron is interfering with itself since each electron was only sent through one at a time—there were no other electrons to interfere with it!\n\nWhen the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons instead of photons, the relevant wavelength is the de Broglie wavelength \\(\\lambda:\\)\n\n\\[\\lambda = \\frac{h}{p},\\]\n\nwhere \\(h\\) is Planck's constant and \\(p\\) is the electron's momentum. \n\n\\[1.5 \\times 10^{-11}\\text{ m}\\]  \\[7.3 \\times 10^{-11}\\text{ m}\\]  \\[1.5 \\times 10^{-10}\\text{ m}\\]  \\[7.3 \\times 10^{-10}\\text{ m}\\] \n\nCalculate the de Broglie wavelength of an electron moving with velocity \\(1.0 \\times 10^{7} \\text{ m/s}.\\)\n\nSubmit your answer\n\nUsain Bolt, the world champion sprinter, hit a top speed of 27.79 miles per hour at the Olympics. If he has a mass of 94 kg, what was his de Broglie wavelength?\n\nExpress your answer as an order of magnitude in units of the Bohr radius \\(r_{B} = 5.29 \\times 10^{-11} \\text{m}\\). For instance, if your answer was \\(4 \\times 10^{-5} r_{B}\\), your should give \\(-5.\\)\n\n[Image 3] \n\n* * *\n\n###### Image Credit: Flickr drcliffordchoi.\n\nWhile the de Broglie relation was postulated for massive matter, the equation applies equally well to light. Given light of a certain wavelength, the momentum and energy of that light can be found using de Broglie's formula. This generalizes the naive formula \\(p = m v\\), which can't be applied to light since light has no mass and always moves at a constant velocity of \\(c\\) regardless of wavelength.\n\n## Modeling the Double-slit Experiment\n\nThe below is reproduced from the 【32†Amplitude, Frequency, Wave Number, Phase Shift】 wiki.\n\nIn Young's double-slit experiment, photons corresponding to light of wavelength \\(\\lambda\\) are fired at a barrier with two thin slits separated by a distance \\(d,\\) as shown in the diagram below. After passing through the slits, they hit a screen at a distance of \\(D\\) away with \\(D \\gg d,\\) and the point of impact is measured. Remarkably, both the experiment and theory of 【13†quantum mechanics】 predict that the number of photons measured at each point along the screen follows a complicated series of peaks and troughs called an interference pattern as below. The photons must exhibit the wave behavior of a relative phase shift somehow to be responsible for this phenomenon. Below, the condition for which maxima of the interference pattern occur on the screen is derived.\n\n[Image 4: Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. ] Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. 【33†[3]】\n\nSince \\(D \\gg d\\), the angle from each of the slits is approximately the same and equal to \\(\\theta\\). If \\(y\\) is the vertical displacement to an interference peak from the midpoint between the slits, it is therefore true that\n\n\\[D\\tan \\theta \\approx D\\sin \\theta \\approx D\\theta = y.\\]\n\nFurthermore, there is a path difference \\(\\Delta L\\) between the two slits and the interference peak. Light from the lower slit must travel \\(\\Delta L\\) further to reach any particular spot on the screen, as in the diagram below:\n\n[Image 5: Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.] Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.\n\nThe condition for constructive interference is that the path difference \\(\\Delta L\\) is exactly equal to an integer number of wavelengths. The phase shift of light traveling over an integer \\(n\\) number of wavelengths is exactly \\(2\\pi n\\), which is",

【196†Bohr】\\n  * 【197†Born】\\n  * 【198†Bose】\\n  * 【199†de Broglie】\\n  * 【200†Compton】\\n  * 【201†Dirac】\\n  * 【202†Davisson】\\n  * 【203†Debye】\\n  * 【204†Ehrenfest】\\n  * 【205†Einstein】\\n  * 【206†Everett】\\n  * 【207†Fock】\\n  * 【208†Fermi】\\n  * 【209†Feynman】\\n  * 【210†Glauber】\\n  * 【211†Gutzwiller】\\n  * 【212†Heisenberg】\\n  * 【213†Hilbert】\\n  * 【214†Jordan】\\n  * 【215†Kramers】\\n  * 【216†Pauli】\\n  * 【217†Lamb】\\n  * 【218†Landau】\\n  * 【219†Laue】\\n  * 【220†Moseley】\\n  * 【221†Millikan】\\n  * 【222†Onnes】\\n  * 【223†Planck】\\n  * 【224†Rabi】\\n  * 【225†Raman】\\n  * 【226†Rydberg】\\n  * 【227†Schrödinger】\\n  * 【228†Simmons】\\n  * 【229†Sommerfeld】\\n  * 【230†von Neumann】\\n  * 【231†Weyl】\\n  * 【232†Wien】\\n  * 【233†Wigner】\\n  * 【234†Zeeman】\\n  * 【235†Zeilinger】\\n\\n  * 【236†v】\\n  * 【237†t】\\n  * 【238†e】\\n\\nIn the basic version of this experiment, a 【239†coherent light source】, such as a 【240†laser】 beam, illuminates a plate pierced by two parallel slits, and the light passing through the slits is observed on a screen behind the plate.【241†[8]】【242†[9]】 The wave nature of light causes the light waves passing through the two slits to 【129†interfere】, producing bright and dark bands on the screen – a result that would not be expected if light consisted of classical particles.【241†[8]】【243†[10]】 However, the light is always found to be absorbed at the screen at discrete points, as individual particles (not waves); the interference pattern appears via the varying density of these particle hits on the screen.【244†[11]】 Furthermore, versions of the experiment that include detectors at the slits find that each detected 【245†photon】 passes through one slit (as would a classical particle), and not through both slits (as would a wave).【246†[12]】【247†[13]】【248†[14]】【249†[15]】【250†[16]】 However, 【251†such experiments】 demonstrate that particles do not form the interference pattern if one detects which slit they pass through. These results demonstrate the principle of 【111†wave–particle duality】.【252†[17]】【253†[18]】\\n\\nOther atomic-scale entities, such as 【254†electrons】, are found to exhibit the same behavior when fired towards a double slit.【242†[9]】 Additionally, the detection of individual discrete impacts is observed to be inherently probabilistic, which is inexplicable using 【125†classical mechanics】.【242†[9]】\\n\\nThe experiment can be done with entities much larger than electrons and photons, although it becomes more difficult as size increases. The largest entities for which the double-slit experiment has been performed were 【255†molecules】 that each comprised 2000 atoms (whose total mass was 25,000 【256†atomic mass units】).【257†[19]】\\n\\nThe double-slit experiment (and its variations) has become a classic for its clarity in expressing the central puzzles of quantum mechanics. Because it demonstrates the fundamental limitation of the ability of the observer to predict experimental results, 【209†Richard Feynman】 called it \\\"a phenomenon which is impossible […] to explain in any 【125†classical way】, and which has in it the heart of quantum mechanics. In reality, it contains the only mystery [of quantum mechanics].\\\"【242†[9]】\\n\\n## Overview[【258†edit】]\\n\\n[Image 3]Same double-slit assembly (0.7 mm between slits); in top image, one slit is closed. In the single-slit image, a 【259†diffraction pattern】 (the faint spots on either side of the main band) forms due to the nonzero width of the slit. This diffraction pattern is also seen in the double-slit image, but with many smaller interference fringes.\\n\\nIf light consisted strictly of ordinary or 【125†classical】 particles, and these particles were fired in a straight line through a slit and allowed to strike a screen on the other side, we would expect to see a pattern corresponding to the size and shape of the slit. However, when this \\\"single-slit experiment\\\" is actually performed, the pattern on the screen is a 【259†diffraction pattern】 in which the light is spread out. The smaller the slit, the greater the angle of spread. - Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "4f5d6013-29b2-4864-b1e1-3f0356946911", "parent_id": "e2dc9d54-d95a-4aed-806b-ff4dec74895a", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:15:58.767335+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"finish_details\":{\"type\":\"stop\",\"stop_tokens\":[100260]},\"citations\":[{\"start_ix\":672,\"end_ix\":683,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"metadata\":{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Which-way detector unlocks some mystery of the double-slit experiment\",\"url\":\"https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html\",\"text\":\"\\nThroughout the years, scientists have demonstrated different versions of the two-slit experiment. In the new study, physicists Stefano Frabboni from the University of Modena and Reggio Emilia and the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience in Modena, Italy; Gian Carlo Gazzadi from the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience; and Giulio Pozzi from the University of Bologna have presented another version of the two-slit experiment using a transmission electron microscope.\\n\\n“Over the last few years, we tried to use our expertise in transmission electron microscopy and focused ion beam specimen preparation to realize some basic experiments related to some of the ‘mysteries’ of quantum mechanics, as pointed out by Feynman in his celebrated lectures and books,” Frabboni told PhysOrg.com.\\n\\nFirst, the scientists used focused ion beam milling to make two nanoslits on a barrier. Then they modified one of the slits by covering it with a filter made of several layers of “low atomic number” material to create a which-way detector for the electrons passing through.\\n\\nAlthough the electrons (which were shot one by one) could still pass through the filtered slit, the filter caused more of the electrons to undergo inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering. As the physicists explained, an electron undergoing inelastic scattering is localized at the covered slit, and acts like a spherical wave after passing through the slit. In contrast, an electron passing through the unfiltered slit is more likely to undergo elastic scattering, and act like a cylindrical wave after passing through that slit. The spherical wave and cylindrical wave do not have any phase correlation, and so even if an electron passed through both slits, the two different waves that come out cannot create an interference pattern on the wall behind them.\\n\\nThe physicists also found that the thickness of the filter determined the interference effects: the thicker the filter, the greater the probability for inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering, and so the fewer the interference effects. They could make the filter thick enough so that the interference effects canceled out almost completely.\\n\\n“When the electron suffers inelastic scattering, it is localized; this means that its wavefunction collapses and after the measurement act, it propagates roughly as a spherical wave from the region of interaction, with no phase relation at all with other elastically or inelastically scattered electrons,” Frabboni said. “The experimental results show electrons through two slits (so two bright lines in the image when elastic and inelastic scattered electrons are collected) with negligible interference effects in the one-slit Fraunhofer diffraction pattern formed with elastic electrons.”\\n\\nIn a separate study, the physicists covered both slits to see if two spherical waves would create an interference pattern. They found that, in the very faint inelastic intensity, no fringes seem present, whereas interference fringes are recovered, at a very low intensity, when the elastic image is taken.\\n\\nOverall, the results suggest that the type of scattering an electron undergoes determines the mark it leaves on the back wall, and that a detector at one of the slits can change the type of scattering. The physicists concluded that, while elastically scattered electrons can cause an interference pattern, the inelastically scattered electrons do not contribute to the interference process.\\n\\nMore information: Stefano Frabboni, Gian Carlo Gazzadi, and Giulio Pozzi. “Ion and electron beam nanofabrication of the which-way double-slit experiment in a transmission electron microscope.” Applied Physics Letters 97, 263101 (2010). 【26†DOI:10.1063/1.3529947†dx.doi.org】 \\n\\nCopyright 2010 PhysOrg.com.  \\nAll rights reserved. Apart from any fair dealing for the purpose of private study or research, no part may be reproduced without the written permission. For general inquiries, please use our 【62†contact form†sciencex.com】. For general feedback, use the public comments section below (please 【63†adhere to guidelines†sciencex.com】). \\n\\nPlease select the most appropriate category to facilitate processing of your request\\n\\n  -- please select one --  Compliments / Critique T\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":{\"cited_message_idx\":13,\"evidence_text\":\"source\"}}},{\"start_ix\":1134,\"end_ix\":1145,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"metadata\":{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Double-slit Experiment | Brilliant Math & Science Wiki\",\"url\":\"https://brilliant.org/wiki/double-slit-experiment/\",\"text\":\"\\nThe original double-slit experiment was performed using light/photons around the turn of the nineteenth century by Thomas Young, so the original experiment is often called Young's double-slit experiment. The idea of using particles other than photons in the experiment did not come until after the ideas of de Broglie and the advent of quantum mechanics, when it was proposed that fundamental particles might also behave as waves with characteristic wavelengths depending on their momenta. The single-electron version of the experiment was in fact not performed until 1974. A more recent version of the experiment successfully demonstrating wave-particle duality used 【20†buckminsterfullerene】 or buckyballs, the \\\\(C_{60}\\\\) 【21†allotrope】 of carbon.\\n\\n#### Contents\\n\\n  * 【22†Waves vs. Particles】 \\n  * 【23†Double-slit Experiment with Electrons】 \\n  * 【24†Modeling the Double-slit Experiment】 \\n  * 【25†References】 \\n\\n## Waves vs. Particles\\n\\nTo understand why the double-slit experiment is important, it is useful to understand the strong distinctions between wave and particles that make wave-particle duality so intriguing.\\n\\nWaves describe oscillating values of a physical quantity that obey the 【26†wave equation】. They are usually described by sums of 【27†sine and cosine】 functions, since any periodic (oscillating) function may be decomposed into a 【28†Fourier series】. When two waves pass through each other, the resulting wave is the sum of the two original waves. This is called a superposition since the waves are placed (\\\"-position\\\") on top of each other (\\\"super-\\\"). Superposition is one of the most fundamental principles of quantum mechanics. A general quantum system need not be in one state or another but can reside in a superposition of two where there is some probability of 【29†measuring】 the quantum 【29†wavefunction】 in one state or another.\\n\\n[Image 1: Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.] Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.【30†[2]】\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 2 \\\\sin (2x)\\\\). The addition of two waves to form a wave of larger amplitude is in general known as constructive interference since the interference results in a larger wave.\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 0\\\\) \\\\(\\\\big(\\\\)since \\\\(\\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi) = - \\\\sin (2x)\\\\big).\\\\) This is known as destructive interference in general, when adding two waves results in a wave of smaller amplitude. See the figure above for examples of both constructive and destructive interference.\\n\\na) only  a) and b)  b) and c)  c) only \\n\\n[Image 2] \\n\\nTwo speakers are generating sounds with the same phase, amplitude, and wavelength. The two sound waves can make constructive interference, as above left. Or they can make destructive interference, as above right. If we want to find out the exact position where the two sounds make destructive interference, which of the following do we need to know?\\n\\na) the wavelength of the sound waves   \\nb) the distances from the two speakers   \\nc) the speed of sound generated by the two speakers\\n\\nThis wave behavior is quite unlike the behavior of particles. Classically, particles are objects with a single definite position and a single definite momentum. Particles do not make interference patterns with other particles in detectors whether or not they pass through slits. They only interact by 【31†colliding elastically】, i.e., via electromagnetic forces at short distances. Before the discovery of quantum mechanics, it was assumed that waves and particles were two distinct models for objects, and that any real physical thing could only be described as a particle or as a wave, but not both.\\n\\n## Double-slit Experiment with Electrons\\n\\nIn the more modern version of the double slit experiment using electrons, electrons with the same momentum are shot from an \\\"electron gun\\\" like the ones inside CRT televisions towards a screen with two slits in it. After each electron goes through one of the slits, it is observed hitting a single point on a detecting screen at an apparently random location. As more and more electrons pass through, one at a time, they form an overall pattern of light and dark interference bands. If each electron was truly just a point particle, then there would only be two clusters of observations: one for the electrons passing through the left slit, and one for the right. However, if electrons are made of waves, they interfere with themselves and pass through both slits simultaneously. Indeed, this is what is observed when the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons. It must therefore be true that the electron is interfering with itself since each electron was only sent through one at a time—there were no other electrons to interfere with it!\\n\\nWhen the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons instead of photons, the relevant wavelength is the de Broglie wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda:\\\\)\\n\\n\\\\[\\\\lambda = \\\\frac{h}{p},\\\\]\\n\\nwhere \\\\(h\\\\) is Planck's constant and \\\\(p\\\\) is the electron's momentum. \\n\\n\\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\] \\n\\nCalculate the de Broglie wavelength of an electron moving with velocity \\\\(1.0 \\\\times 10^{7} \\\\text{ m/s}.\\\\)\\n\\nSubmit your answer\\n\\nUsain Bolt, the world champion sprinter, hit a top speed of 27.79 miles per hour at the Olympics. If he has a mass of 94 kg, what was his de Broglie wavelength?\\n\\nExpress your answer as an order of magnitude in units of the Bohr radius \\\\(r_{B} = 5.29 \\\\times 10^{-11} \\\\text{m}\\\\). For instance, if your answer was \\\\(4 \\\\times 10^{-5} r_{B}\\\\), your should give \\\\(-5.\\\\)\\n\\n[Image 3] \\n\\n* * *\\n\\n###### Image Credit: Flickr drcliffordchoi.\\n\\nWhile the de Broglie relation was postulated for massive matter, the equation applies equally well to light. Given light of a certain wavelength, the momentum and energy of that light can be found using de Broglie's formula. This generalizes the naive formula \\\\(p = m v\\\\), which can't be applied to light since light has no mass and always moves at a constant velocity of \\\\(c\\\\) regardless of wavelength.\\n\\n## Modeling the Double-slit Experiment\\n\\nThe below is reproduced from the 【32†Amplitude, Frequency, Wave Number, Phase Shift】 wiki.\\n\\nIn Young's double-slit experiment, photons corresponding to light of wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda\\\\) are fired at a barrier with two thin slits separated by a distance \\\\(d,\\\\) as shown in the diagram below. After passing through the slits, they hit a screen at a distance of \\\\(D\\\\) away with \\\\(D \\\\gg d,\\\\) and the point of impact is measured. Remarkably, both the experiment and theory of 【13†quantum mechanics】 predict that the number of photons measured at each point along the screen follows a complicated series of peaks and troughs called an interference pattern as below. The photons must exhibit the wave behavior of a relative phase shift somehow to be responsible for this phenomenon. Below, the condition for which maxima of the interference pattern occur on the screen is derived.\\n\\n[Image 4: Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. ] Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. 【33†[3]】\\n\\nSince \\\\(D \\\\gg d\\\\), the angle from each of the slits is approximately the same and equal to \\\\(\\\\theta\\\\). If \\\\(y\\\\) is the vertical displacement to an interference peak from the midpoint between the slits, it is therefore true that\\n\\n\\\\[D\\\\tan \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\sin \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\theta = y.\\\\]\\n\\nFurthermore, there is a path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) between the two slits and the interference peak. Light from the lower slit must travel \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) further to reach any particular spot on the screen, as in the diagram below:\\n\\n[Image 5: Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.] Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.\\n\\nThe condition for constructive interference is that the path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) is exactly equal to an integer number of wavelengths. The phase shift of light traveling over an integer \\\\(n\\\\) number of wavelengths is exactly \\\\(2\\\\pi n\\\\), which is\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":{\"cited_message_idx\":14,\"evidence_text\":\"source\"}}},{\"start_ix\":1145,\"end_ix\":1156,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"metadata\":{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Double-slit paradox】\\n  * 【186†Density matrix】\\n  * 【187†Scattering theory】\\n  * 【188†Quantum statistical mechanics】\\n  * 【189†Quantum machine learning】\\n\\nScientists\\n\\n  * 【190†Aharonov】\\n  * 【191†Bell】\\n  * 【192†Bethe】\\n  * 【193†Blackett】\\n  * 【194†Bloch】\\n  * 【195†Bohm】\\n  * 【196†Bohr】\\n  * 【197†Born】\\n  * 【198†Bose】\\n  * 【199†de Broglie】\\n  * 【200†Compton】\\n  * 【201†Dirac】\\n  * 【202†Davisson】\\n  * 【203†Debye】\\n  * 【204†Ehrenfest】\\n  * 【205†Einstein】\\n  * 【206†Everett】\\n  * 【207†Fock】\\n  * 【208†Fermi】\\n  * 【209†Feynman】\\n  * 【210†Glauber】\\n  * 【211†Gutzwiller】\\n  * 【212†Heisenberg】\\n  * 【213†Hilbert】\\n  * 【214†Jordan】\\n  * 【215†Kramers】\\n  * 【216†Pauli】\\n  * 【217†Lamb】\\n  * 【218†Landau】\\n  * 【219†Laue】\\n  * 【220†Moseley】\\n  * 【221†Millikan】\\n  * 【222†Onnes】\\n  * 【223†Planck】\\n  * 【224†Rabi】\\n  * 【225†Raman】\\n  * 【226†Rydberg】\\n  * 【227†Schrödinger】\\n  * 【228†Simmons】\\n  * 【229†Sommerfeld】\\n  * 【230†von Neumann】\\n  * 【231†Weyl】\\n  * 【232†Wien】\\n  * 【233†Wigner】\\n  * 【234†Zeeman】\\n  * 【235†Zeilinger】\\n\\n  * 【236†v】\\n  * 【237†t】\\n  * 【238†e】\\n\\nIn the basic version of this experiment, a 【239†coherent light source】, such as a 【240†laser】 beam, illuminates a plate pierced by two parallel slits, and the light passing through the slits is observed on a screen behind the plate.【241†[8]】【242†[9]】 The wave nature of light causes the light waves passing through the two slits to 【129†interfere】, producing bright and dark bands on the screen – a result that would not be expected if light consisted of classical particles.【241†[8]】【243†[10]】 However, the light is always found to be absorbed at the screen at discrete points, as individual particles (not waves); the interference pattern appears via the varying density of these particle hits on the screen.【244†[11]】 Furthermore, versions of the experiment that include detectors at the slits find that each detected 【245†photon】 passes through one slit (as would a classical particle), and not through both slits (as would a wave).【246†[12]】【247†[13]】【248†[14]】【249†[15]】【250†[16]】 However, 【251†such experiments】 demonstrate that particles do not form the interference pattern if one detects which slit they pass through. These results demonstrate the principle of 【111†wave–particle duality】.【252†[17]】【253†[18]】\\n\\nOther atomic-scale entities, such as 【254†electrons】, are found to exhibit the same behavior when fired towards a double slit.【242†[9]】 Additionally, the detection of individual discrete impacts is observed to be inherently probabilistic, which is inexplicable using 【125†classical mechanics】.【242†[9]】\\n\\nThe experiment can be done with entities much larger than electrons and photons, although it becomes more difficult as size increases. The largest entities for which the double-slit experiment has been performed were 【255†molecules】 that each comprised 2000 atoms (whose total mass was 25,000 【256†atomic mass units】).【257†[19]】\\n\\nThe double-slit experiment (and its variations) has become a classic for its clarity in expressing the central puzzles of quantum mechanics. Because it demonstrates the fundamental limitation of the ability of the observer to predict experimental results, 【209†Richard Feynman】 called it \\\"a phenomenon which is impossible […] to explain in any 【125†classical way】, and which has in it the heart of quantum mechanics. In reality, it contains the only mystery [of quantum mechanics].\\\"【242†[9]】\\n\\n## Overview[【258†edit】]\\n\\n[Image 3]Same double-slit assembly (0.7 mm between slits); in top image, one slit is closed. In the single-slit image, a 【259†diffraction pattern】 (the faint spots on either side of the main band) forms due to the nonzero width of the slit. This diffraction pattern is also seen in the double-slit image, but with many smaller interference fringes.\\n\\nIf light consisted strictly of ordinary or 【125†classical】 particles, and these particles were fired in a straight line through a slit and allowed to strike a screen on the other side, we would expect to see a pattern corresponding to the size and shape of the slit. However, when this \\\"single-slit experiment\\\" is actually performed, the pattern on the screen is a 【259†diffraction pattern】 in which the light is spread out. The smaller the slit, the greater the angle of spread. The top porti\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":{\"cited_message_idx\":15,\"evidence_text\":\"source\"}}}],\"is_complete\":true,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4\",\"voice_mode_message\":true,\"parent_id\":\"e2dc9d54-d95a-4aed-806b-ff4dec74895a\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\",\"content_references\":[{\"matched_text\":\"【13†source】\",\"start_idx\":672,\"end_idx\":683,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"webpage_extended\",\"title\":\"Which-way detector unlocks some mystery of the double-slit experiment\",\"url\":\"https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html\",\"pub_date\":null,\"snippet\":\"\\nThroughout the years, scientists have demonstrated different versions of the two-slit experiment. In the new study, physicists Stefano Frabboni from the University of Modena and Reggio Emilia and the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience in Modena, Italy; Gian Carlo Gazzadi from the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience; and Giulio Pozzi from the University of Bologna have presented another version of the two-slit experiment using a transmission electron microscope.\\n\\n“Over the last few years, we tried to use our expertise in transmission electron microscopy and focused ion beam specimen preparation to realize some basic experiments related to some of the ‘mysteries’ of quantum mechanics, as pointed out by Feynman in his celebrated lectures and books,” Frabboni told PhysOrg.com.\\n\\nFirst, the scientists used focused ion beam milling to make two nanoslits on a barrier. Then they modified one of the slits by covering it with a filter made of several layers of “low atomic number” material to create a which-way detector for the electrons passing through.\\n\\nAlthough the electrons (which were shot one by one) could still pass through the filtered slit, the filter caused more of the electrons to undergo inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering. As the physicists explained, an electron undergoing inelastic scattering is localized at the covered slit, and acts like a spherical wave after passing through the slit. In contrast, an electron passing through the unfiltered slit is more likely to undergo elastic scattering, and act like a cylindrical wave after passing through that slit. The spherical wave and cylindrical wave do not have any phase correlation, and so even if an electron passed through both slits, the two different waves that come out cannot create an interference pattern on the wall behind them.\\n\\nThe physicists also found that the thickness of the filter determined the interference effects: the thicker the filter, the greater the probability for inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering, and so the fewer the interference effects. They could make the filter thick enough so that the interference effects canceled out almost completely.\\n\\n“When the electron suffers inelastic scattering, it is localized; this means that its wavefunction collapses and after the measurement act, it propagates roughly as a spherical wave from the region of interaction, with no phase relation at all with other elastically or inelastically scattered electrons,” Frabboni said. “The experimental results show electrons through two slits (so two bright lines in the image when elastic and inelastic scattered electrons are collected) with negligible interference effects in the one-slit Fraunhofer diffraction pattern formed with elastic electrons.”\\n\\nIn a separate study, the physicists covered both slits to see if two spherical waves would create an interference pattern. They found that, in the very faint inelastic intensity, no fringes seem present, whereas interference fringes are recovered, at a very low intensity, when the elastic image is taken.\\n\\nOverall, the results suggest that the type of scattering an electron undergoes determines the mark it leaves on the back wall, and that a detector at one of the slits can change the type of scattering. The physicists concluded that, while elastically scattered electrons can cause an interference pattern, the inelastically scattered electrons do not contribute to the interference process.\\n\\nMore information: Stefano Frabboni, Gian Carlo Gazzadi, and Giulio Pozzi. “Ion and electron beam nanofabrication of the which-way double-slit experiment in a transmission electron microscope.” Applied Physics Letters 97, 263101 (2010). 【26†DOI:10.1063/1.3529947†dx.doi.org】 \\n\\nCopyright 2010 PhysOrg.com.  \\nAll rights reserved. For general feedback, use the public comments section below (please 【63†adhere to guidelines†sciencex.com】). \\n\\nPlease select the most appropriate category to facilitate processing of your request\\n\\n  -- please select one --  Compliments / Critique T\",\"attribution\":\"phys.org\",\"icon_type\":null},{\"matched_text\":\"【14†source】\",\"start_idx\":1134,\"end_idx\":1145,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"webpage_extended\",\"title\":\"Double-slit Experiment | Brilliant Math & Science Wiki\",\"url\":\"https://brilliant.org/wiki/double-slit-experiment/\",\"pub_date\":null,\"snippet\":\"\\nThe original double-slit experiment was performed using light/photons around the turn of the nineteenth century by Thomas Young, so the original experiment is often called Young's double-slit experiment. The idea of using particles other than photons in the experiment did not come until after the ideas of de Broglie and the advent of quantum mechanics, when it was proposed that fundamental particles might also behave as waves with characteristic wavelengths depending on their momenta. The single-electron version of the experiment was in fact not performed until 1974. A more recent version of the experiment successfully demonstrating wave-particle duality used 【20†buckminsterfullerene】 or buckyballs, the \\\\(C_{60}\\\\) 【21†allotrope】 of carbon.\\n\\n#### Contents\\n\\n  * 【22†Waves vs. Particles】 \\n  * 【23†Double-slit Experiment with Electrons】 \\n  * 【24†Modeling the Double-slit Experiment】 \\n  * 【25†References】 \\n\\n## Waves vs. Particles\\n\\nTo understand why the double-slit experiment is important, it is useful to understand the strong distinctions between wave and particles that make wave-particle duality so intriguing.\\n\\nWaves describe oscillating values of a physical quantity that obey the 【26†wave equation】. They are usually described by sums of 【27†sine and cosine】 functions, since any periodic (oscillating) function may be decomposed into a 【28†Fourier series】. When two waves pass through each other, the resulting wave is the sum of the two original waves. This is called a superposition since the waves are placed (\\\"-position\\\") on top of each other (\\\"super-\\\"). Superposition is one of the most fundamental principles of quantum mechanics. A general quantum system need not be in one state or another but can reside in a superposition of two where there is some probability of 【29†measuring】 the quantum 【29†wavefunction】 in one state or another.\\n\\n[Image 1: Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.] Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.【30†[2]】\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 2 \\\\sin (2x)\\\\). The addition of two waves to form a wave of larger amplitude is in general known as constructive interference since the interference results in a larger wave.\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 0\\\\) \\\\(\\\\big(\\\\)since \\\\(\\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi) = - \\\\sin (2x)\\\\big).\\\\) This is known as destructive interference in general, when adding two waves results in a wave of smaller amplitude. See the figure above for examples of both constructive and destructive interference.\\n\\na) only  a) and b)  b) and c)  c) only \\n\\n[Image 2] \\n\\nTwo speakers are generating sounds with the same phase, amplitude, and wavelength. The two sound waves can make constructive interference, as above left. Or they can make destructive interference, as above right. If we want to find out the exact position where the two sounds make destructive interference, which of the following do we need to know?\\n\\na) the wavelength of the sound waves   \\nb) the distances from the two speakers   \\nc) the speed of sound generated by the two speakers\\n\\nThis wave behavior is quite unlike the behavior of particles. Classically, particles are objects with a single definite position and a single definite momentum. Particles do not make interference patterns with other particles in detectors whether or not they pass through slits. They only interact by 【31†colliding elastically】, i.e., via electromagnetic forces at short distances. Before the discovery of quantum mechanics, it was assumed that waves and particles were two distinct models for objects, and that any real physical thing could only be described as a particle or as a wave, but not both.\\n\\n## Double-slit Experiment with Electrons\\n\\nIn the more modern version of the double slit experiment using electrons, electrons with the same momentum are shot from an \\\"electron gun\\\" like the ones inside CRT televisions towards a screen with two slits in it. After each electron goes through one of the slits, it is observed hitting a single point on a detecting screen at an apparently random location. As more and more electrons pass through, one at a time, they form an overall pattern of light and dark interference bands. If each electron was truly just a point particle, then there would only be two clusters of observations: one for the electrons passing through the left slit, and one for the right. However, if electrons are made of waves, they interfere with themselves and pass through both slits simultaneously. Indeed, this is what is observed when the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons. It must therefore be true that the electron is interfering with itself since each electron was only sent through one at a time—there were no other electrons to interfere with it!\\n\\nWhen the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons instead of photons, the relevant wavelength is the de Broglie wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda:\\\\)\\n\\n\\\\[\\\\lambda = \\\\frac{h}{p},\\\\]\\n\\nwhere \\\\(h\\\\) is Planck's constant and \\\\(p\\\\) is the electron's momentum. \\n\\n\\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\] \\n\\nCalculate the de Broglie wavelength of an electron moving with velocity \\\\(1.0 \\\\times 10^{7} \\\\text{ m/s}.\\\\)\\n\\nSubmit your answer\\n\\nUsain Bolt, the world champion sprinter, hit a top speed of 27.79 miles per hour at the Olympics. If he has a mass of 94 kg, what was his de Broglie wavelength?\\n\\nExpress your answer as an order of magnitude in units of the Bohr radius \\\\(r_{B} = 5.29 \\\\times 10^{-11} \\\\text{m}\\\\). For instance, if your answer was \\\\(4 \\\\times 10^{-5} r_{B}\\\\), your should give \\\\(-5.\\\\)\\n\\n[Image 3] \\n\\n* * *\\n\\n###### Image Credit: Flickr drcliffordchoi.\\n\\nWhile the de Broglie relation was postulated for massive matter, the equation applies equally well to light. Given light of a certain wavelength, the momentum and energy of that light can be found using de Broglie's formula. This generalizes the naive formula \\\\(p = m v\\\\), which can't be applied to light since light has no mass and always moves at a constant velocity of \\\\(c\\\\) regardless of wavelength.\\n\\n## Modeling the Double-slit Experiment\\n\\nThe below is reproduced from the 【32†Amplitude, Frequency, Wave Number, Phase Shift】 wiki.\\n\\nIn Young's double-slit experiment, photons corresponding to light of wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda\\\\) are fired at a barrier with two thin slits separated by a distance \\\\(d,\\\\) as shown in the diagram below. After passing through the slits, they hit a screen at a distance of \\\\(D\\\\) away with \\\\(D \\\\gg d,\\\\) and the point of impact is measured. Remarkably, both the experiment and theory of 【13†quantum mechanics】 predict that the number of photons measured at each point along the screen follows a complicated series of peaks and troughs called an interference pattern as below. The photons must exhibit the wave behavior of a relative phase shift somehow to be responsible for this phenomenon. Below, the condition for which maxima of the interference pattern occur on the screen is derived.\\n\\n[Image 4: Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. ] Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. 【33†[3]】\\n\\nSince \\\\(D \\\\gg d\\\\), the angle from each of the slits is approximately the same and equal to \\\\(\\\\theta\\\\). If \\\\(y\\\\) is the vertical displacement to an interference peak from the midpoint between the slits, it is therefore true that\\n\\n\\\\[D\\\\tan \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\sin \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\theta = y.\\\\]\\n\\nFurthermore, there is a path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) between the two slits and the interference peak. Light from the lower slit must travel \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) further to reach any particular spot on the screen, as in the diagram below:\\n\\n[Image 5: Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.] Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.\\n\\nThe condition for constructive interference is that the path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) is exactly equal to an integer number of wavelengths. The phase shift of light traveling over an integer \\\\(n\\\\) number of wavelengths is exactly \\\\(2\\\\pi n\\\\), which is\",\"attribution\":\"brilliant.org\",\"icon_type\":null},{\"matched_text\":\"【15†source】\",\"start_idx\":1145,\"end_idx\":1156,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"webpage_extended\",\"title\":\"Double-slit 【232†Wien】\\n  * 【233†Wigner】\\n  * 【234†Zeeman】\\n  * 【235†Zeilinger】\\n\\n  * 【236†v】\\n  * 【237†t】\\n  * 【238†e】\\n\\nIn the basic version of this experiment, a 【239†coherent light source】, such as a 【240†laser】 beam, illuminates a plate pierced by two parallel slits, and the light passing through the slits is observed on a screen behind the plate.【241†[8]】【242†[9]】 The wave nature of light causes the light waves passing through the two slits to 【129†interfere】, producing bright and dark bands on the screen – a result that would not be expected if light consisted of classical particles.【241†[8]】【243†[10]】 However, the light is always found to be absorbed at the screen at discrete points, as individual particles (not waves); the interference pattern appears via the varying density of these particle hits on the screen.【244†[11]】 Furthermore, versions of the experiment that include detectors at the slits find that each detected 【245†photon】 passes through one slit (as would a classical particle), and not through both slits (as would a wave).【246†[12]】【247†[13]】【248†[14]】【249†[15]】【250†[16]】 However, 【251†such experiments】 demonstrate that particles do not form the interference pattern if one detects which slit they pass through. These results demonstrate the principle of 【111†wave–particle duality】.【252†[17]】【253†[18]】\\n\\nOther atomic-scale entities, such as 【254†electrons】, are found to exhibit the same behavior when fired towards a double slit.【242†[9]】 Additionally, the detection of individual discrete impacts is observed to be inherently probabilistic, which is inexplicable using 【125†classical mechanics】.【242†[9]】\\n\\nThe experiment can be done with entities much larger than electrons and photons, although it becomes more difficult as size increases. The largest entities for which the double-slit experiment has been performed were 【255†molecules】 that each comprised 2000 atoms (whose total mass was 25,000 【256†atomic mass units】).【257†[19]】\\n\\nThe double-slit experiment (and its variations) has become a classic for its clarity in expressing the central puzzles of quantum mechanics. Because it demonstrates the fundamental limitation of the ability of the observer to predict experimental results, 【209†Richard Feynman】 called it \\\"a phenomenon which is impossible […] to explain in any 【125†classical way】, and which has in it the heart of quantum mechanics. In reality, it contains the only mystery [of quantum mechanics].\\\"【242†[9]】\\n\\n## Overview[【258†edit】]\\n\\n[Image 3]Same double-slit assembly (0.7 mm between slits); in top image, one slit is closed. In the single-slit image, a 【259†diffraction pattern】 (the faint spots on either side of the main band) forms due to the nonzero width of the slit. This diffraction pattern is also seen in the double-slit image, but with many smaller interference fringes.\\n\\nIf light consisted strictly of ordinary or 【125†classical】 particles, and these particles were fired in a straight line through a slit and allowed to strike a screen on the other side, we would expect to see a pattern corresponding to the size and shape of the slit. However, when this \\\"single-slit experiment\\\" is actually performed, the pattern on the screen is a 【259†diffraction pattern】 in which the light is spread out. The smaller the slit, the greater the angle of spread. The top porti\",\"attribution\":\"en.wikipedia.org\",\"icon_type\":null}]}"}`


The experiment you're referring to sounds like a version of the double-slit experiment, a fundamental demonstration in quantum mechanics. This experiment has been conducted in various forms to explore the wave-particle duality of electrons and other particles.

In one version, scientists used a transmission electron microscope to perform the experiment. They created two nanoslits on a barrier and observed how electrons behaved as they passed through these slits. The experiment was designed to explore the mysteries of quantum mechanics and particularly focused on how the electrons' behavior changed depending on whether they underwent inelastic or elastic scattering【13†source】.

The double-slit experiment originally used light or photons and was later adapted to use electrons. When electrons pass through the slits, they create an interference pattern on a detecting screen, demonstrating wave-like behavior. This outcome was surprising because if electrons were purely particles, they would form two distinct clusters on the screen. However, the interference pattern suggests that electrons have wave-like properties as well【14†source】【15†source】.

These experiments are crucial in understanding quantum mechanics and the nature of particles at a fundamental level. They show how particles like electrons can display characteristics of both particles and waves, depending on the experimental setup. This duality is central to many concepts in quantum physics.
