- Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "ae6d2092-0493-4a9f-a098-e0d0ff37c485", "parent_id": "318533f0-c66e-4d2f-a9df-6102a0d27e93", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:18:35.526321+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"finish_details\":{\"type\":\"stop\",\"stop_tokens\":[100260]},\"citations\":[{\"start_ix\":307,\"end_ix\":314,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"metadata\":{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Which-way detector unlocks some mystery of the double-slit experiment\",\"url\":\"https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html\",\"text\":\"\\nThroughout the years, scientists have demonstrated different versions of the two-slit experiment. In the new study, physicists Stefano Frabboni from the University of Modena and Reggio Emilia and the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience in Modena, Italy; Gian Carlo Gazzadi from the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience; and Giulio Pozzi from the University of Bologna have presented another version of the two-slit experiment using a transmission electron microscope.\\n\\n“Over the last few years, we tried to use our expertise in transmission electron microscopy and focused ion beam specimen preparation to realize some basic experiments related to some of the ‘mysteries’ of quantum mechanics, as pointed out by Feynman in his celebrated lectures and books,” Frabboni told PhysOrg.com.\\n\\nFirst, the scientists used focused ion beam milling to make two nanoslits on a barrier. Then they modified one of the slits by covering it with a filter made of several layers of “low atomic number” material to create a which-way detector for the electrons passing through.\\n\\nAlthough the electrons (which were shot one by one) could still pass through the filtered slit, the filter caused more of the electrons to undergo inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering. As the physicists explained, an electron undergoing inelastic scattering is localized at the covered slit, and acts like a spherical wave after passing through the slit. In contrast, an electron passing through the unfiltered slit is more likely to undergo elastic scattering, and act like a cylindrical wave after passing through that slit. The spherical wave and cylindrical wave do not have any phase correlation, and so even if an electron passed through both slits, the two different waves that come out cannot create an interference pattern on the wall behind them.\\n\\nThe physicists also found that the thickness of the filter determined the interference effects: the thicker the filter, the greater the probability for inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering, and so the fewer the interference effects. They could make the filter thick enough so that the interference effects canceled out almost completely.\\n\\n“When the electron suffers inelastic scattering, it is localized; this means that its wavefunction collapses and after the measurement act, it propagates roughly as a spherical wave from the region of interaction, with no phase relation at all with other elastically or inelastically scattered electrons,” Frabboni said. “The experimental results show electrons through two slits (so two bright lines in the image when elastic and inelastic scattered electrons are collected) with negligible interference effects in the one-slit Fraunhofer diffraction pattern formed with elastic electrons.”\\n\\nIn a separate study, the physicists covered both slits to see if two spherical waves would create an interference pattern. They found that, in the very faint inelastic intensity, no fringes seem present, whereas interference fringes are recovered, at a very low intensity, when the elastic image is taken.\\n\\nOverall, the results suggest that the type of scattering an electron undergoes determines the mark it leaves on the back wall, and that a detector at one of the slits can change the type of scattering. The physicists concluded that, while elastically scattered electrons can cause an interference pattern, the inelastically scattered electrons do not contribute to the interference process.\\n\\nMore information: Stefano Frabboni, Gian Carlo Gazzadi, and Giulio Pozzi. “Ion and electron beam nanofabrication of the which-way double-slit experiment in a transmission electron microscope.” Applied Physics Letters 97, 263101 (2010). 【26†DOI:10.1063/1.3529947†dx.doi.org】 \\n\\nCopyright 2010 PhysOrg.com.  \\nAll rights reserved. Apart from any fair dealing for the purpose of private study or research, no part may be reproduced without the written permission. For general inquiries, please use our 【62†contact form†sciencex.com】. For general feedback, use the public comments section below (please 【63†adhere to guidelines†sciencex.com】). \\n\\nPlease select the most appropriate category to facilitate processing of your request\\n\\n  -- please select one --  Compliments / Critique T\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":{\"cited_message_idx\":13,\"evidence_text\":\"출처\"}}},{\"start_ix\":537,\"end_ix\":544,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"metadata\":{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Double-slit Experiment | Brilliant Math & Science Wiki\",\"url\":\"https://brilliant.org/wiki/double-slit-experiment/\",\"text\":\"\\nThe original double-slit experiment was performed using light/photons around the turn of the nineteenth century by Thomas Young, so the original experiment is often called Young's double-slit experiment. The idea of using particles other than photons in the experiment did not come until after the ideas of de Broglie and the advent of quantum mechanics, when it was proposed that fundamental particles might also behave as waves with characteristic wavelengths depending on their momenta. The single-electron version of the experiment was in fact not performed until 1974. A more recent version of the experiment successfully demonstrating wave-particle duality used 【20†buckminsterfullerene】 or buckyballs, the \\\\(C_{60}\\\\) 【21†allotrope】 of carbon.\\n\\n#### Contents\\n\\n  * 【22†Waves vs. Particles】 \\n  * 【23†Double-slit Experiment with Electrons】 \\n  * 【24†Modeling the Double-slit Experiment】 \\n  * 【25†References】 \\n\\n## Waves vs. Particles\\n\\nTo understand why the double-slit experiment is important, it is useful to understand the strong distinctions between wave and particles that make wave-particle duality so intriguing.\\n\\nWaves describe oscillating values of a physical quantity that obey the 【26†wave equation】. They are usually described by sums of 【27†sine and cosine】 functions, since any periodic (oscillating) function may be decomposed into a 【28†Fourier series】. When two waves pass through each other, the resulting wave is the sum of the two original waves. This is called a superposition since the waves are placed (\\\"-position\\\") on top of each other (\\\"super-\\\"). Superposition is one of the most fundamental principles of quantum mechanics. A general quantum system need not be in one state or another but can reside in a superposition of two where there is some probability of 【29†measuring】 the quantum 【29†wavefunction】 in one state or another.\\n\\n[Image 1: Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.] Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.【30†[2]】\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 2 \\\\sin (2x)\\\\). The addition of two waves to form a wave of larger amplitude is in general known as constructive interference since the interference results in a larger wave.\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 0\\\\) \\\\(\\\\big(\\\\)since \\\\(\\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi) = - \\\\sin (2x)\\\\big).\\\\) This is known as destructive interference in general, when adding two waves results in a wave of smaller amplitude. See the figure above for examples of both constructive and destructive interference.\\n\\na) only  a) and b)  b) and c)  c) only \\n\\n[Image 2] \\n\\nTwo speakers are generating sounds with the same phase, amplitude, and wavelength. The two sound waves can make constructive interference, as above left. Or they can make destructive interference, as above right. If we want to find out the exact position where the two sounds make destructive interference, which of the following do we need to know?\\n\\na) the wavelength of the sound waves   \\nb) the distances from the two speakers   \\nc) the speed of sound generated by the two speakers\\n\\nThis wave behavior is quite unlike the behavior of particles. Classically, particles are objects with a single definite position and a single definite momentum. Particles do not make interference patterns with other particles in detectors whether or not they pass through slits. They only interact by 【31†colliding elastically】, i.e., via electromagnetic forces at short distances. Before the discovery of quantum mechanics, it was assumed that waves and particles were two distinct models for objects, and that any real physical thing could only be described as a particle or as a wave, but not both.\\n\\n## Double-slit Experiment with Electrons\\n\\nIn the more modern version of the double slit experiment using electrons, electrons with the same momentum are shot from an \\\"electron gun\\\" like the ones inside CRT televisions towards a screen with two slits in it. After each electron goes through one of the slits, it is observed hitting a single point on a detecting screen at an apparently random location. As more and more electrons pass through, one at a time, they form an overall pattern of light and dark interference bands. If each electron was truly just a point particle, then there would only be two clusters of observations: one for the electrons passing through the left slit, and one for the right. However, if electrons are made of waves, they interfere with themselves and pass through both slits simultaneously. Indeed, this is what is observed when the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons. It must therefore be true that the electron is interfering with itself since each electron was only sent through one at a time—there were no other electrons to interfere with it!\\n\\nWhen the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons instead of photons, the relevant wavelength is the de Broglie wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda:\\\\)\\n\\n\\\\[\\\\lambda = \\\\frac{h}{p},\\\\]\\n\\nwhere \\\\(h\\\\) is Planck's constant and \\\\(p\\\\) is the electron's momentum. \\n\\n\\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\] \\n\\nCalculate the de Broglie wavelength of an electron moving with velocity \\\\(1.0 \\\\times 10^{7} \\\\text{ m/s}.\\\\)\\n\\nSubmit your answer\\n\\nUsain Bolt, the world champion sprinter, hit a top speed of 27.79 miles per hour at the Olympics. If he has a mass of 94 kg, what was his de Broglie wavelength?\\n\\nExpress your answer as an order of magnitude in units of the Bohr radius \\\\(r_{B} = 5.29 \\\\times 10^{-11} \\\\text{m}\\\\). For instance, if your answer was \\\\(4 \\\\times 10^{-5} r_{B}\\\\), your should give \\\\(-5.\\\\)\\n\\n[Image 3] \\n\\n* * *\\n\\n###### Image Credit: Flickr drcliffordchoi.\\n\\nWhile the de Broglie relation was postulated for massive matter, the equation applies equally well to light. Given light of a certain wavelength, the momentum and energy of that light can be found using de Broglie's formula. This generalizes the naive formula \\\\(p = m v\\\\), which can't be applied to light since light has no mass and always moves at a constant velocity of \\\\(c\\\\) regardless of wavelength.\\n\\n## Modeling the Double-slit Experiment\\n\\nThe below is reproduced from the 【32†Amplitude, Frequency, Wave Number, Phase Shift】 wiki.\\n\\nIn Young's double-slit experiment, photons corresponding to light of wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda\\\\) are fired at a barrier with two thin slits separated by a distance \\\\(d,\\\\) as shown in the diagram below. After passing through the slits, they hit a screen at a distance of \\\\(D\\\\) away with \\\\(D \\\\gg d,\\\\) and the point of impact is measured. Remarkably, both the experiment and theory of 【13†quantum mechanics】 predict that the number of photons measured at each point along the screen follows a complicated series of peaks and troughs called an interference pattern as below. The photons must exhibit the wave behavior of a relative phase shift somehow to be responsible for this phenomenon. Below, the condition for which maxima of the interference pattern occur on the screen is derived.\\n\\n[Image 4: Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. ] Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. 【33†[3]】\\n\\nSince \\\\(D \\\\gg d\\\\), the angle from each of the slits is approximately the same and equal to \\\\(\\\\theta\\\\). If \\\\(y\\\\) is the vertical displacement to an interference peak from the midpoint between the slits, it is therefore true that\\n\\n\\\\[D\\\\tan \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\sin \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\theta = y.\\\\]\\n\\nFurthermore, there is a path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) between the two slits and the interference peak. Light from the lower slit must travel \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) further to reach any particular spot on the screen, as in the diagram below:\\n\\n[Image 5: Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.] Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.\\n\\nThe condition for constructive interference is that the path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) is exactly equal to an integer number of wavelengths. The phase shift of light traveling over an integer \\\\(n\\\\) number of wavelengths is exactly \\\\(2\\\\pi n\\\\), which is\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":{\"cited_message_idx\":14,\"evidence_text\":\"출처\"}}},{\"start_ix\":544,\"end_ix\":551,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"metadata\":{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Double-slit experiment - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double-slit_experiment\",\"text\":\"\\nPart of a series of articles about  \\n【101†Quantum mechanics】  \\n[Image 2: {\\\\displaystyle i\\\\hbar {\\\\frac {\\\\partial }{\\\\partial t}}|\\\\psi (t)\\\\rangle ={\\\\hat {H}}|\\\\psi (t)\\\\rangle }]\\n\\n【121†Schrödinger equation】  \\n\\n  * 【122†Introduction】\\n  * 【123†Glossary】\\n 【173†Superdeterminism】\\n  * 【174†Many-worlds】\\n  * 【175†Objective collapse】\\n  * 【176†Quantum logic】\\n  * 【177†Relational】\\n  * 【178†Transactional】\\n  * 【179†Von Neumann–Wigner】\\n\\nAdvanced topics\\n\\n  * 【180†Relativistic quantum mechanics】\\n  * 【181†Quantum field theory】\\n  * 【182†Quantum information science】\\n  * 【183†Quantum computing】\\n  * 【184†Quantum chaos】\\n  * 【185†EPR paradox】\\n  * 【186†Density matrix】\\n  * 【187†Scattering theory】\\n  * 【188†Quantum statistical mechanics】\\n  * 【189†Quantum machine learning】\\n\\nScientists\\n\\n  * 【190†Aharonov】\\n  * 【191†Bell】\\n  * 【192†Bethe】\\n  * 【193†Blackett】\\n  * 【194†Bloch】\\n  * 【195†Bohm】\\n  * 【196†Bohr】\\n  * 【197†Born】\\n  * 【198†Bose】\\n  * 【199†de Broglie】\\n  * 【200†Compton】\\n  * 【201†Dirac】\\n  * 【202†Davisson】\\n  * 【203†Debye】\\n  * 【204†Ehrenfest】\\n  * 【205†Einstein】\\n  * 【206†Everett】\\n  * 【207†Fock】\\n  * 【208†Fermi】\\n  * 【209†Feynman】\\n  * 【210†Glauber】\\n  * 【211†Gutzwiller】\\n  * 【212†Heisenberg】\\n  * 【213†Hilbert】\\n  * 【214†Jordan】\\n  * 【215†Kramers】\\n  * 【216†Pauli】\\n  * 【217†Lamb】\\n  * 【218†Landau】\\n  * 【219†Laue】\\n  * 【220†Moseley】\\n  * 【221†Millikan】\\n  * 【222†Onnes】\\n  * 【223†Planck】\\n  * 【224†Rabi】\\n  * 【225†Raman】\\n  * 【226†Rydberg】\\n  * 【227†Schrödinger】\\n  * 【228†Simmons】\\n  * 【229†Sommerfeld】\\n  * 【230†von Neumann】\\n  * 【231†Weyl】\\n  * 【232†Wien】\\n  * 【233†Wigner】\\n  * 【234†Zeeman】\\n  * 【235†Zeilinger】\\n\\n  * 【236†v】\\n  * 【237†t】\\n  * 【238†e】\\n\\nIn the basic version of this experiment, a 【239†coherent light source】, such as a 【240†laser】 beam, illuminates a plate pierced by two parallel slits, and the light passing through the slits is observed on a screen behind the plate.【241†[8]】【242†[9]】 The wave nature of light causes the light waves passing through the two slits to 【129†interfere】, producing bright and dark bands on the screen – a result that would not be expected if light consisted of classical particles.【241†[8]】【243†[10]】 However, the light is always found to be absorbed at the screen at discrete points, as individual particles (not waves); the interference pattern appears via the varying density of these particle hits on the screen.【244†[11]】 Furthermore, versions of the experiment that include detectors at the slits find that each detected 【245†photon】 passes through one slit (as would a classical particle), and not through both slits (as would a wave).【246†[12]】【247†[13]】【248†[14]】【249†[15]】【250†[16]】 However, 【251†such experiments】 demonstrate that particles do not form the interference pattern if one detects which slit they pass through. These results demonstrate the principle of 【111†wave–particle duality】.【252†[17]】【253†[18]】\\n\\nOther atomic-scale entities, such as 【254†electrons】, are found to exhibit the same behavior when fired towards a double slit.【242†[9]】 Additionally, the detection of individual discrete impacts is observed to be inherently probabilistic, which is inexplicable using 【125†classical mechanics】.【242†[9]】\\n\\nThe experiment can be done with entities much larger than electrons and photons, although it becomes more difficult as size increases. The largest entities for which the double-slit experiment has been performed were 【255†molecules】 that each comprised 2000 atoms (whose total mass was 25,000 【256†atomic mass units】).【257†[19]】\\n\\nThe double-slit experiment (and its variations) has become a classic for its clarity in expressing the central puzzles of quantum mechanics. Because it demonstrates the fundamental limitation of the ability of the observer to predict experimental results, 【209†Richard Feynman】 called it \\\"a phenomenon which is impossible […] to explain in any 【125†classical way】, and which has in it the heart of quantum mechanics. In reality, it contains the only mystery [of quantum mechanics].\\\"【242†[9]】\\n\\n## Overview[【258†edit】]\\n\\n[Image 3]Same double-slit assembly (0.7 mm between slits); in top image, one slit is closed. In the single-slit image, a 【259†diffraction pattern】 (the faint spots on either side of the main band) forms due to the nonzero width of the slit. This diffraction pattern is also seen in the double-slit image, but with many smaller interference fringes.\\n\\nIf light consisted strictly of ordinary or 【125†classical】 particles, and these particles were fired in a straight line through a slit and allowed to strike a screen on the other side, we would expect to see a pattern corresponding to the size and shape of the slit. However, when this \\\"single-slit experiment\\\" is actually performed, the pattern on the screen is a 【259†diffraction pattern】 in which the light is spread out. The smaller the slit, the greater the angle of spread. The top porti\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":{\"cited_message_idx\":15,\"evidence_text\":\"출처\"}}}],\"is_complete\":true,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4\",\"parent_id\":\"318533f0-c66e-4d2f-a9df-6102a0d27e93\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\",\"content_references\":[{\"matched_text\":\"【13†출처】\",\"start_idx\":307,\"end_idx\":314,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"webpage_extended\",\"title\":\"Which-way detector unlocks some mystery of the double-slit experiment\",\"url\":\"https://phys.org/news/2011-01-which-way-detector-mystery-double-slit.html\",\"pub_date\":null,\"snippet\":\"\\nThroughout the years, scientists have demonstrated different versions of the two-slit experiment. In the new study, physicists Stefano Frabboni from the University of Modena and Reggio Emilia and the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience in Modena, Italy; Gian Carlo Gazzadi from the CNR-Institute of Nanoscience; and Giulio Pozzi from the University of Bologna have presented another version of the two-slit experiment using a transmission electron microscope.\\n\\n“Over the last few years, we tried to use our expertise in transmission electron microscopy and focused ion beam specimen preparation to realize some basic experiments related to some of the ‘mysteries’ of quantum mechanics, as pointed out by Feynman in his celebrated lectures and books,” Frabboni told PhysOrg.com.\\n\\nFirst, the scientists used focused ion beam milling to make two nanoslits on a barrier. Then they modified one of the slits by covering it with a filter made of several layers of “low atomic number” material to create a which-way detector for the electrons passing through.\\n\\nAlthough the electrons (which were shot one by one) could still pass through the filtered slit, the filter caused more of the electrons to undergo inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering. As the physicists explained, an electron undergoing inelastic scattering is localized at the covered slit, and acts like a spherical wave after passing through the slit. In contrast, an electron passing through the unfiltered slit is more likely to undergo elastic scattering, and act like a cylindrical wave after passing through that slit. The spherical wave and cylindrical wave do not have any phase correlation, and so even if an electron passed through both slits, the two different waves that come out cannot create an interference pattern on the wall behind them.\\n\\nThe physicists also found that the thickness of the filter determined the interference effects: the thicker the filter, the greater the probability for inelastic scattering rather than elastic scattering, and so the fewer the interference effects. They could make the filter thick enough so that the interference effects canceled out almost completely.\\n\\n“When the electron suffers inelastic scattering, it is localized; this means that its wavefunction collapses and after the measurement act, it propagates roughly as a spherical wave from the region of interaction, with no phase relation at all with other elastically or inelastically scattered electrons,” Frabboni said. “The experimental results show electrons through two slits (so two bright lines in the image when elastic and inelastic scattered electrons are collected) with negligible interference effects in the one-slit Fraunhofer diffraction pattern formed with elastic electrons.”\\n\\nIn a separate study, the physicists covered both slits to see if two spherical waves would create an interference pattern. They found that, in the very faint inelastic intensity, no fringes seem present, whereas interference fringes are recovered, at a very low intensity, when the elastic image is taken.\\n\\nOverall, the results suggest that the type of scattering an electron undergoes determines the mark it leaves on the back wall, and that a detector at one of the slits can change the type of scattering. The physicists concluded that, while elastically scattered electrons can cause an interference pattern, the inelastically scattered electrons do not contribute to the interference process.\\n\\nMore information: Stefano Frabboni, Gian Carlo Gazzadi, and Giulio Pozzi. “Ion and electron beam nanofabrication of the which-way double-slit experiment in a transmission electron microscope.” Applied Physics Letters 97, 263101 (2010). 【26†DOI:10.1063/1.3529947†dx.doi.org】 \\n\\nCopyright 2010 PhysOrg.com.  \\nAll rights reserved. For general feedback, use the public comments section below (please 【63†adhere to guidelines†sciencex.com】). \\n\\nPlease select the most appropriate category to facilitate processing of your request\\n\\n  -- please select one --  Compliments / Critique T\",\"attribution\":\"phys.org\",\"icon_type\":null},{\"matched_text\":\"【14†출처】\",\"start_idx\":537,\"end_idx\":544,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"webpage_extended\",\"title\":\"Double-slit Experiment | Brilliant Math & Science Wiki\",\"url\":\"https://brilliant.org/wiki/double-slit-experiment/\",\"pub_date\":null,\"snippet\":\"\\nThe original double-slit experiment was performed using light/photons around the turn of the nineteenth century by Thomas Young, so the original experiment is often called Young's double-slit experiment. The idea of using particles other than photons in the experiment did not come until after the ideas of de Broglie and the advent of quantum mechanics, when it was proposed that fundamental particles might also behave as waves with characteristic wavelengths depending on their momenta. The single-electron version of the experiment was in fact not performed until 1974. A more recent version of the experiment successfully demonstrating wave-particle duality used 【20†buckminsterfullerene】 or buckyballs, the \\\\(C_{60}\\\\) 【21†allotrope】 of carbon.\\n\\n#### Contents\\n\\n  * 【22†Waves vs. Particles】 \\n  * 【23†Double-slit Experiment with Electrons】 \\n  * 【24†Modeling the Double-slit Experiment】 \\n  * 【25†References】 \\n\\n## Waves vs. Particles\\n\\nTo understand why the double-slit experiment is important, it is useful to understand the strong distinctions between wave and particles that make wave-particle duality so intriguing.\\n\\nWaves describe oscillating values of a physical quantity that obey the 【26†wave equation】. They are usually described by sums of 【27†sine and cosine】 functions, since any periodic (oscillating) function may be decomposed into a 【28†Fourier series】. When two waves pass through each other, the resulting wave is the sum of the two original waves. This is called a superposition since the waves are placed (\\\"-position\\\") on top of each other (\\\"super-\\\"). Superposition is one of the most fundamental principles of quantum mechanics. A general quantum system need not be in one state or another but can reside in a superposition of two where there is some probability of 【29†measuring】 the quantum 【29†wavefunction】 in one state or another.\\n\\n[Image 1: Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.] Left: example of superposed waves constructively interfering. Right: superposed waves destructively interfering.【30†[2]】\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 2 \\\\sin (2x)\\\\). The addition of two waves to form a wave of larger amplitude is in general known as constructive interference since the interference results in a larger wave.\\n\\nIf one wave is \\\\(A(x) = \\\\sin (2x)\\\\) and the other is \\\\(B(x) = \\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi)\\\\), then they add together to make \\\\(A + B = 0\\\\) \\\\(\\\\big(\\\\)since \\\\(\\\\sin (2x + \\\\pi) = - \\\\sin (2x)\\\\big).\\\\) This is known as destructive interference in general, when adding two waves results in a wave of smaller amplitude. See the figure above for examples of both constructive and destructive interference.\\n\\na) only  a) and b)  b) and c)  c) only \\n\\n[Image 2] \\n\\nTwo speakers are generating sounds with the same phase, amplitude, and wavelength. The two sound waves can make constructive interference, as above left. Or they can make destructive interference, as above right. If we want to find out the exact position where the two sounds make destructive interference, which of the following do we need to know?\\n\\na) the wavelength of the sound waves   \\nb) the distances from the two speakers   \\nc) the speed of sound generated by the two speakers\\n\\nThis wave behavior is quite unlike the behavior of particles. Classically, particles are objects with a single definite position and a single definite momentum. Particles do not make interference patterns with other particles in detectors whether or not they pass through slits. They only interact by 【31†colliding elastically】, i.e., via electromagnetic forces at short distances. Before the discovery of quantum mechanics, it was assumed that waves and particles were two distinct models for objects, and that any real physical thing could only be described as a particle or as a wave, but not both.\\n\\n## Double-slit Experiment with Electrons\\n\\nIn the more modern version of the double slit experiment using electrons, electrons with the same momentum are shot from an \\\"electron gun\\\" like the ones inside CRT televisions towards a screen with two slits in it. After each electron goes through one of the slits, it is observed hitting a single point on a detecting screen at an apparently random location. As more and more electrons pass through, one at a time, they form an overall pattern of light and dark interference bands. If each electron was truly just a point particle, then there would only be two clusters of observations: one for the electrons passing through the left slit, and one for the right. However, if electrons are made of waves, they interfere with themselves and pass through both slits simultaneously. Indeed, this is what is observed when the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons. It must therefore be true that the electron is interfering with itself since each electron was only sent through one at a time—there were no other electrons to interfere with it!\\n\\nWhen the double-slit experiment is performed using electrons instead of photons, the relevant wavelength is the de Broglie wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda:\\\\)\\n\\n\\\\[\\\\lambda = \\\\frac{h}{p},\\\\]\\n\\nwhere \\\\(h\\\\) is Planck's constant and \\\\(p\\\\) is the electron's momentum. \\n\\n\\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-11}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[1.5 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\]  \\\\[7.3 \\\\times 10^{-10}\\\\text{ m}\\\\] \\n\\nCalculate the de Broglie wavelength of an electron moving with velocity \\\\(1.0 \\\\times 10^{7} \\\\text{ m/s}.\\\\)\\n\\nSubmit your answer\\n\\nUsain Bolt, the world champion sprinter, hit a top speed of 27.79 miles per hour at the Olympics. If he has a mass of 94 kg, what was his de Broglie wavelength?\\n\\nExpress your answer as an order of magnitude in units of the Bohr radius \\\\(r_{B} = 5.29 \\\\times 10^{-11} \\\\text{m}\\\\). For instance, if your answer was \\\\(4 \\\\times 10^{-5} r_{B}\\\\), your should give \\\\(-5.\\\\)\\n\\n[Image 3] \\n\\n* * *\\n\\n###### Image Credit: Flickr drcliffordchoi.\\n\\nWhile the de Broglie relation was postulated for massive matter, the equation applies equally well to light. Given light of a certain wavelength, the momentum and energy of that light can be found using de Broglie's formula. This generalizes the naive formula \\\\(p = m v\\\\), which can't be applied to light since light has no mass and always moves at a constant velocity of \\\\(c\\\\) regardless of wavelength.\\n\\n## Modeling the Double-slit Experiment\\n\\nThe below is reproduced from the 【32†Amplitude, Frequency, Wave Number, Phase Shift】 wiki.\\n\\nIn Young's double-slit experiment, photons corresponding to light of wavelength \\\\(\\\\lambda\\\\) are fired at a barrier with two thin slits separated by a distance \\\\(d,\\\\) as shown in the diagram below. After passing through the slits, they hit a screen at a distance of \\\\(D\\\\) away with \\\\(D \\\\gg d,\\\\) and the point of impact is measured. Remarkably, both the experiment and theory of 【13†quantum mechanics】 predict that the number of photons measured at each point along the screen follows a complicated series of peaks and troughs called an interference pattern as below. The photons must exhibit the wave behavior of a relative phase shift somehow to be responsible for this phenomenon. Below, the condition for which maxima of the interference pattern occur on the screen is derived.\\n\\n[Image 4: Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. ] Left: actual experimental two-slit interference pattern of photons, exhibiting many small peaks and troughs. Right: schematic diagram of the experiment as described above. 【33†[3]】\\n\\nSince \\\\(D \\\\gg d\\\\), the angle from each of the slits is approximately the same and equal to \\\\(\\\\theta\\\\). If \\\\(y\\\\) is the vertical displacement to an interference peak from the midpoint between the slits, it is therefore true that\\n\\n\\\\[D\\\\tan \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\sin \\\\theta \\\\approx D\\\\theta = y.\\\\]\\n\\nFurthermore, there is a path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) between the two slits and the interference peak. Light from the lower slit must travel \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) further to reach any particular spot on the screen, as in the diagram below:\\n\\n[Image 5: Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.] Light from the lower slit must travel further to reach the screen at any given point above the midpoint, causing the interference pattern.\\n\\nThe condition for constructive interference is that the path difference \\\\(\\\\Delta L\\\\) is exactly equal to an integer number of wavelengths. The phase shift of light traveling over an integer \\\\(n\\\\) number of wavelengths is exactly \\\\(2\\\\pi n\\\\), which is\",\"attribution\":\"brilliant.org\",\"icon_type\":null},{\"matched_text\":\"【15†출처】\",\"start_idx\":544,\"end_idx\":551,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"webpage_extended\",\"title\":\"Double-slit experiment 【222†Onnes】\\n  * 【223†Planck】\\n  * 【224†Rabi】\\n  * 【225†Raman】\\n  * 【226†Rydberg】\\n  * 【227†Schrödinger】\\n  * 【228†Simmons】\\n  * 【229†Sommerfeld】\\n  * 【230†von Neumann】\\n  * 【231†Weyl】\\n  * 【232†Wien】\\n  * 【233†Wigner】\\n  * 【234†Zeeman】\\n  * 【235†Zeilinger】\\n\\n  * 【236†v】\\n  * 【237†t】\\n  * 【238†e】\\n\\nIn the basic version of this experiment, a 【239†coherent light source】, such as a 【240†laser】 beam, illuminates a plate pierced by two parallel slits, and the light passing through the slits is observed on a screen behind the plate.【241†[8]】【242†[9]】 The wave nature of light causes the light waves passing through the two slits to 【129†interfere】, producing bright and dark bands on the screen – a result that would not be expected if light consisted of classical particles.【241†[8]】【243†[10]】 However, the light is always found to be absorbed at the screen at discrete points, as individual particles (not waves); the interference pattern appears via the varying density of these particle hits on the screen.【244†[11]】 Furthermore, versions of the experiment that include detectors at the slits find that each detected 【245†photon】 passes through one slit (as would a classical particle), and not through both slits (as would a wave).【246†[12]】【247†[13]】【248†[14]】【249†[15]】【250†[16]】 However, 【251†such experiments】 demonstrate that particles do not form the interference pattern if one detects which slit they pass through. These results demonstrate the principle of 【111†wave–particle duality】.【252†[17]】【253†[18]】\\n\\nOther atomic-scale entities, such as 【254†electrons】, are found to exhibit the same behavior when fired towards a double slit.【242†[9]】 Additionally, the detection of individual discrete impacts is observed to be inherently probabilistic, which is inexplicable using 【125†classical mechanics】.【242†[9]】\\n\\nThe experiment can be done with entities much larger than electrons and photons, although it becomes more difficult as size increases. The largest entities for which the double-slit experiment has been performed were 【255†molecules】 that each comprised 2000 atoms (whose total mass was 25,000 【256†atomic mass units】).【257†[19]】\\n\\nThe double-slit experiment (and its variations) has become a classic for its clarity in expressing the central puzzles of quantum mechanics. Because it demonstrates the fundamental limitation of the ability of the observer to predict experimental results, 【209†Richard Feynman】 called it \\\"a phenomenon which is impossible […] to explain in any 【125†classical way】, and which has in it the heart of quantum mechanics. In reality, it contains the only mystery [of quantum mechanics].\\\"【242†[9]】\\n\\n## Overview[【258†edit】]\\n\\n[Image 3]Same double-slit assembly (0.7 mm between slits); in top image, one slit is closed. In the single-slit image, a 【259†diffraction pattern】 (the faint spots on either side of the main band) forms due to the nonzero width of the slit. This diffraction pattern is also seen in the double-slit image, but with many smaller interference fringes.\\n\\nIf light consisted strictly of ordinary or 【125†classical】 particles, and these particles were fired in a straight line through a slit and allowed to strike a screen on the other side, we would expect to see a pattern corresponding to the size and shape of the slit. However, when this \\\"single-slit experiment\\\" is actually performed, the pattern on the screen is a 【259†diffraction pattern】 in which the light is spread out. The smaller the slit, the greater the angle of spread. The top porti\",\"attribution\":\"en.wikipedia.org\",\"icon_type\":null}]}"}`


물리학에서의 이중 슬릿 실험은 양자역학의 핵심 개념을 보여주는 실험 중 하나입니다. 이 실험은 원래 광자나 빛을 사용하여 처음 수행되었고, 후에 전자와 같은 다른 입자들을 사용하여 변형되었습니다.

이 실험의 한 버전에서는, 과학자들이 전송 전자 현미경을 사용하여 실험을 수행했습니다. 그들은 장벽에 두 개의 나노 슬릿을 만들고 전자가 이 슬릿을 통과할 때 어떻게 행동하는지 관찰했습니다. 이 실험은 양자역학의 미스터리를 탐구하는 데 초점을 맞추었으며, 특히 전자가 비탄성 또는 탄성 산란을 겪었을 때 그 행동이 어떻게 달라지는지에 대해 연구했습니다【13†출처】.

이중 슬릿 실험은 원래 빛 또는 광자를 사용하여 수행되었으며 나중에 전자를 사용하여 적용되었습니다. 전자가 슬릿을 통과할 때, 그들은 검출 화면에 간섭 무늬를 생성하며, 이는 파동과 같은 행동을 보여줍니다. 이 결과는 놀라웠는데, 전자가 순수한 입자라면 스크린에 두 개의 명확한 군집을 형성할 것으로 예상되었기 때문입니다. 그러나 간섭 무늬는 전자가 파동과 같은 특성을 가지고 있음을 시사합니다【14†출처】【15†출처】.

이 실험들은 양자역학과 기본적인 입자의 본성을 이해하는 데 중요합니다. 이들은 실험 설정에 따라 입자와 파동의 특성을 모두 보여줄 수 있는 전자와 같은 입자의 특성을 보여줍니다. 이러한 이중성은 양자 물리학의 많은 개념에서 중심적인 역할을 합니다.

### Message 23 — tool

- Timestamp: 2023-12-27T01:19:37.055612+00:00
- Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "37eb7776-cccf-4dbb-b9db-dfe6414addd1", "parent_id": "66b9258e-dd80-4587-b521-0a2d23e37573", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:19:37.055612+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"_cite_metadata\":{\"citation_format\":{\"name\":\"tether_og\"},\"metadata_list\":[{\"type\":\"webpage\",\"title\":\"Time domain double slit interference of electron produced by XUV synchrotron radiation | Scientific Reports\",\"url\":\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-023-33039-9\",\"text\":\"\\n【64†Full size image】\\n\\nA pair of light wave packets sequentially interacts with a helium atom, producing a pair of photoelectron wave packets that propagate in free space (Fig. 【63†1】b). Due to the momentum dispersion of the matter waves, the first and second components of the photoelectron wave packet pair temporally spread in free space and overlap each other, leading to the appearance of the interference pattern in coordinate space【50†15】,【51†16】. This wave packet interference gives rise to the appearance of interference fringes in energy or momentum distributions. Therefore, the measurement of the photoelectron spectrum makes the observation of wave packet interference experimentally possible. Figure 【63†1】c shows a schematic drawing of the energy level of helium atom and the ionization process using a pair of light wave packets. Note that in the actual experiment, an incoherent mixture of numerous pairs of light wave packets in the radiation pulse interacts with a helium atom, leading to the production of pairs of photoelectron wave packets that are randomly distributed in a temporal duration of 300 ps. However, the incoherent properties among the photoelectron wave packets related to different light wave packets are canceled out in the observable quantities [see Methods]. Consequently, the interference pattern observed in this study directly reflects the quantum interference between photoelectron wave packets produced by a pair of light wave packets.\\n\\nIn order to visualize the buildup of the interference pattern, we observe the wave packet interference in the energy-domain. We record photoelectron images using a two-dimensional (2D) position-sensitive detector mounted in a hemispherical electron energy analyzer as shown in Fig. 【63†1】b. The 2D detector consists of a micro-channel plate (MCP), a phosphor screen, and a charge-coupled device (CCD) camera. We adjust the light intensity to ensure that there is only a single photoelectron in the electron analyzer at any given time, enabling the observation of photoelectron interference. The buildup process of the quantum interference pattern is monitored by accumulating the CCD images.\\n\\n### Buildup of photoelectron interference \\n\\nFigure 【65†2】 compares the CCD images of photoelectrons recorded with the radiation from single- (Fig. 【65†2】a,b) and tandem-undulator configurations (Fig. 【65†2】c). The single-undulator configuration is prepared by setting the pole gap of one of the two undulators to its maximum, where the radiation power of the undulator becomes negligibly small. The field strength of the phase shifter magnet was fixed with the same coil current during the measurement. While the horizontal axis of the image shows the kinetic energy of the photoelectron, the vertical axis represents the ionization points of atoms along the light propagation axis in the gas cell. When the single-undulator is used, the helium atom is ionized by light wave packets emitted from either the upstream or downstream undulator, leading to the production of single (not-paired) photoelectron wave packets. Therefore, no interference pattern can be exhibited on the CCD image. In this case, the photoelectron distribution on the detector plane simply reflects the spectral distribution of the undulator radiation, which is characterized by a 10% width (FWHM) of the central photon energy. With increasing numbers of photoelectrons, broad structures centered at around 4.5 eV become visible in panels of Fig. 【65†2】a,b. On the other hand, interfering photoelectron wave packets are produced when the radiation from the tandem-undulator is used to ionize helium atoms. The CCD images in Fig. 【65†2】c shows the two-dimensional distribution of photoelectrons measured with pairs of light wave packets. The CCD image after accumulation of 2000 electrons shows an almost random distribution. An interference pattern exhibiting the wave-like nature of single photoelectrons appears at 2 × 10^{4} electrons, and becomes clearer as the number of electrons is increased.\\n\\nFigure 2\\n\\n[Image 1: figure 2]\\n\\nOne-by-one detection of single-photoelectrons. (a–c) CCD images of photoelectrons observed for radiation from (a) the upstream undulator, (b) the downstream undulator, and (c) the tandem-undulator. The phase shifter current was kept constant during the measurements. The total number of detected electrons are shown at the left side of the panels (a). The color bar ranges from 0 to 4 electrons in the upper three panels, and from 0 to 80 electrons in the images obtained at 1 × 10^{6} electrons. (d–f) One-dimensional distributions obtained by projecting the electron intensities in the images of 1 × 10^{6} electrons onto the energy axis. The interference fringes were fitted by a model curve using Eq. (【66†3】).\\n\\n【67†Full size image】\\n\\nOne-dimensional photoelectron spectra are obtained from the two-dimensional images at N e ~ 1 × 10^{6} by integrating the photoelectron counts onto the horizontal axis (Fig. 【65†2】d–f). The photoelectron spectra obtained by the single-undulator show single peak structures centered at around 4.5 eV kinetic energy. In contrast, the photoelectron spectrum measured with the tandem-undulator is characterized by an interference pattern. The photoelectron spectra in Fig. 【65†2】d,e can be well fitted by a single Gauss function which approximately represents the spectral distribution of the radiation from the single-undulator. The interference pattern in Fig. 【65†2】f, on the other hand, can be fitted by the probability density for pairs of photoelectron wave packets in the energy-domain (see “【68†Methods】”). In the fitting, the width of the temporal slit determines the envelope of the interference pattern which is experimentally obtained by the fitting for the photoelectron spectra measured with the single-undulator. The free parameters in the fitting were the time delay between the light wave packets and the fringe contrast. The theoretical curve well fits the interference pattern, and time delays are obtained to be 6.9 fs. The contrast of the interference fringes is limited to 0.7, mainly due to the reduction in temporal coherence resulting from electron beam properties such as the energy spread and angular divergence, which smear the time delay【58†23】.\\n\\n### Control of double-slit separation \\n\\nFigure 【69†3】a shows the time-domain interferogram, which consists of photoelectron spectra measured as a function of time delay. The horizontal axis shows the time delay produced by the phase shifter magnet. To obtain the absolute time delay τ, it is required to add a minimum delay of approximately 1.9 fs which corresponds to the sum of the temporal duration of the light wave packet and the time delay due to the slippage effect in the drift space between the two undulators. The vertical axis is the kinetic energy of the photoelectron. The interferogram exhibits a periodical modulation of photoelectron intensity on the two-dimensional plot. Similar to the discussion on the interference between the electron wave packets in the bound state【57†22】, the periodic intensity modulation can be explained by time-domain Ramsey interference between the photoelectron wave packets. When the time delay is varied, constructive and destructive interference occurs between the plane waves which constitute the photoelectron wave packets. The interference condition is determined by the phase difference ωτ. Here, the photon frequency is given by ω = (E + E _{IP})/ħ where E and E _{IP} are the kinetic energy of photoelectron and ionization potential of the atom, respectively【50†15】. Therefore, the photoelectron intensity oscillates at a temporal period of around 140 as. The time-domain Ramsey interference is clearly visible over the whole area of the interferogram, indicating that the photoelectron wave packet interference can be precisely controlled by adjusting the temporal separation between the two light wave packets at the attosecond level.\\n\\nFigure 3\\n\\n[Image 2: figure 3]\\n\\nTime-domain photoelectron interferogram. (a) The photoelectron interferogram consists of photoelectron spectra measured as a function of the phase shifter delay. (b–d) Photoelectron spectra obtained at phase shifter delays of 0.51, 2.50, and 4.52 fs (vertical dashed lines in a). The photoelectron spectra are fitted by Eq. (【66†3】). The experimental data points and fitting curves are shown by gray circles and red curve, respectively, in (b–d).\\n\\n【70†Full size image】\\n\\nFigures 【69†3】b–d show the photoelectron spectra at phase shifter delays of 0.5, 2.5, and 4.5 fs (indicated by vertical dashed lines in a), showing interference patterns dependent on the time delay. The photoelectron spectra in Fig. 【69†3】 are well fitted by Eq. (【66†3】), and the time delays are obtained to be 2.41, 4.40, and 6.41 fs, respectively. The photoelectron spectra exhibit fringe structures that vary with the time delay. This observation is a manifestation of the fact that the temporal separation between the two slits determines the spacing between the inte\",\"pub_date\":null,\"extra\":null}],\"original_query\":null},\"command\":\"mclick\",\"args\":[\"[\\\"8\\\", \\\"10\\\", \\\"13\\\"]\"],\"status\":\"finished\",\"is_complete\":true,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4\",\"parent_id\":\"66b9258e-dd80-4587-b521-0a2d23e37573\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\"}"}`

  "text": "\n【64†Full size image】\n\nA pair of light wave packets sequentially interacts with a helium atom, producing a pair of photoelectron wave packets that propagate in free space (Fig. 【63†1】b). Due to the momentum dispersion of the matter waves, the first and second components of the photoelectron wave packet pair temporally spread in free space and overlap each other, leading to the appearance of the interference pattern in coordinate space【50†15】,【51†16】. This wave packet interference gives rise to the appearance of interference fringes in energy or momentum distributions. Therefore, the measurement of the photoelectron spectrum makes the observation of wave packet interference experimentally possible. Figure 【63†1】c shows a schematic drawing of the energy level of helium atom and the ionization process using a pair of light wave packets. Note that in the actual experiment, an incoherent mixture of numerous pairs of light wave packets in the radiation pulse interacts with a helium atom, leading to the production of pairs of photoelectron wave packets that are randomly distributed in a temporal duration of 300 ps. However, the incoherent properties among the photoelectron wave packets related to different light wave packets are canceled out in the observable quantities [see Methods]. Consequently, the interference pattern observed in this study directly reflects the quantum interference between photoelectron wave packets produced by a pair of light wave packets.\n\nIn order to visualize the buildup of the interference pattern, we observe the wave packet interference in the energy-domain. We record photoelectron images using a two-dimensional (2D) position-sensitive detector mounted in a hemispherical electron energy analyzer as shown in Fig. 【63†1】b. The 2D detector consists of a micro-channel plate (MCP), a phosphor screen, and a charge-coupled device (CCD) camera. We adjust the light intensity to ensure that there is only a single photoelectron in the electron analyzer at any given time, enabling the observation of photoelectron interference. The buildup process of the quantum interference pattern is monitored by accumulating the CCD images.\n\n### Buildup of photoelectron interference \n\nFigure 【65†2】 compares the CCD images of photoelectrons recorded with the radiation from single- (Fig. 【65†2】a,b) and tandem-undulator configurations (Fig. 【65†2】c). The single-undulator configuration is prepared by setting the pole gap of one of the two undulators to its maximum, where the radiation power of the undulator becomes negligibly small. The field strength of the phase shifter magnet was fixed with the same coil current during the measurement. While the horizontal axis of the image shows the kinetic energy of the photoelectron, the vertical axis represents the ionization points of atoms along the light propagation axis in the gas cell. When the single-undulator is used, the helium atom is ionized by light wave packets emitted from either the upstream or downstream undulator, leading to the production of single (not-paired) photoelectron wave packets. Therefore, no interference pattern can be exhibited on the CCD image. In this case, the photoelectron distribution on the detector plane simply reflects the spectral distribution of the undulator radiation, which is characterized by a 10% width (FWHM) of the central photon energy. With increasing numbers of photoelectrons, broad structures centered at around 4.5 eV become visible in panels of Fig. 【65†2】a,b. On the other hand, interfering photoelectron wave packets are produced when the radiation from the tandem-undulator is used to ionize helium atoms. The CCD images in Fig. 【65†2】c shows the two-dimensional distribution of photoelectrons measured with pairs of light wave packets. The CCD image after accumulation of 2000 electrons shows an almost random distribution. An interference pattern exhibiting the wave-like nature of single photoelectrons appears at 2 × 10^{4} electrons, and becomes clearer as the number of electrons is increased.\n\nFigure 2\n\n[Image 1: figure 2]\n\nOne-by-one detection of single-photoelectrons. (a–c) CCD images of photoelectrons observed for radiation from (a) the upstream undulator, (b) the downstream undulator, and (c) the tandem-undulator. The phase shifter current was kept constant during the measurements. The total number of detected electrons are shown at the left side of the panels (a). The color bar ranges from 0 to 4 electrons in the upper three panels, and from 0 to 80 electrons in the images obtained at 1 × 10^{6} electrons. (d–f) One-dimensional distributions obtained by projecting the electron intensities in the images of 1 × 10^{6} electrons onto the energy axis. The interference fringes were fitted by a model curve using Eq. (【66†3】).\n\n【67†Full size image】\n\nOne-dimensional photoelectron spectra are obtained from the two-dimensional images at N e ~ 1 × 10^{6} by integrating the photoelectron counts onto the horizontal axis (Fig. 【65†2】d–f). The photoelectron spectra obtained by the single-undulator show single peak structures centered at around 4.5 eV kinetic energy. In contrast, the photoelectron spectrum measured with the tandem-undulator is characterized by an interference pattern. The photoelectron spectra in Fig. 【65†2】d,e can be well fitted by a single Gauss function which approximately represents the spectral distribution of the radiation from the single-undulator. The interference pattern in Fig. 【65†2】f, on the other hand, can be fitted by the probability density for pairs of photoelectron wave packets in the energy-domain (see “【68†Methods】”). In the fitting, the width of the temporal slit determines the envelope of the interference pattern which is experimentally obtained by the fitting for the photoelectron spectra measured with the single-undulator. The free parameters in the fitting were the time delay between the light wave packets and the fringe contrast. The theoretical curve well fits the interference pattern, and time delays are obtained to be 6.9 fs. The contrast of the interference fringes is limited to 0.7, mainly due to the reduction in temporal coherence resulting from electron beam properties such as the energy spread and angular divergence, which smear the time delay【58†23】.\n\n### Control of double-slit separation \n\nFigure 【69†3】a shows the time-domain interferogram, which consists of photoelectron spectra measured as a function of time delay. The horizontal axis shows the time delay produced by the phase shifter magnet. To obtain the absolute time delay τ, it is required to add a minimum delay of approximately 1.9 fs which corresponds to the sum of the temporal duration of the light wave packet and the time delay due to the slippage effect in the drift space between the two undulators. The vertical axis is the kinetic energy of the photoelectron. The interferogram exhibits a periodical modulation of photoelectron intensity on the two-dimensional plot. Similar to the discussion on the interference between the electron wave packets in the bound state【57†22】, the periodic intensity modulation can be explained by time-domain Ramsey interference between the photoelectron wave packets. When the time delay is varied, constructive and destructive interference occurs between the plane waves which constitute the photoelectron wave packets. The interference condition is determined by the phase difference ωτ. Here, the photon frequency is given by ω = (E + E _{IP})/ħ where E and E _{IP} are the kinetic energy of photoelectron and ionization potential of the atom, respectively【50†15】. Therefore, the photoelectron intensity oscillates at a temporal period of around 140 as. The time-domain Ramsey interference is clearly visible over the whole area of the interferogram, indicating that the photoelectron wave packet interference can be precisely controlled by adjusting the temporal separation between the two light wave packets at the attosecond level.\n\nFigure 3\n\n[Image 2: figure 3]\n\nTime-domain photoelectron interferogram. (a) The photoelectron interferogram consists of photoelectron spectra measured as a function of the phase shifter delay. (b–d) Photoelectron spectra obtained at phase shifter delays of 0.51, 2.50, and 4.52 fs (vertical dashed lines in a). The photoelectron spectra are fitted by Eq. (【66†3】). The experimental data points and fitting curves are shown by gray circles and red curve, respectively, in (b–d).\n\n【70†Full size image】\n\nFigures 【69†3】b–d show the photoelectron spectra at phase shifter delays of 0.5, 2.5, and 4.5 fs (indicated by vertical dashed lines in a), showing interference patterns dependent on the time delay. The photoelectron spectra in Fig. 【69†3】 are well fitted by Eq. (【66†3】), and the time delays are obtained to be 2.41, 4.40, and 6.41 fs, respectively. The photoelectron spectra exhibit fringe structures that vary with the time delay. This observation is a manifestation of the fact that the temporal separation between the two slits determines the spacing between the inte",
  "title": "Time domain double slit interference of electron produced by XUV synchrotron radiation | Scientific Reports",
  "tether_id": null

Could you explain and elaborate?\\n\\nAnswering the question requires a working knowledge of the double slit experiment. Rather than reinvent the wheel, the following video explains the relevant aspects of the experiment well.\\n\\nThe pertinent points are:\\n\\n1. As an electron propagates from the gun to the slit, it exists in a superposition of different states (going through one slit, the other slit, neither slit, or both slits). The interference pattern on the wall confirms this indeterminate state.  \\n2. Researchers measure which slit the electron passed through, which then causes the electron to exist in a definite state (passing through either one slit or the other, but not both).\\n\\nMathematically, physicists describe the initial superposition of states with a 【21†wave function†hyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu】. Yet any specific measurement of the system results in a definite state. Furthermore, mathematical operations on the wave function correctly determine the probability of each of the possible outcomes.\\n\\nThe slit experiment’s results are well established—the question is how to【22†interpret those results†en.wikipedia.org】. An increasingly popular interpretation of the experiment results argues that measurements cause an irreversible interaction with the system that leads to the definite result. Rather than the wave function collapsing, it “decoheres” like a single strand of hair disentangling from a large knot. And this is where the experiment becomes relevant to the multiverse discussion. 【23†Physicist Hugh Everett†en.wikipedia.org】 used this quantum decoherence to develop the 【24†many-worlds interpretation†en.wikipedia.org】 of quantum mechanics. This interpretation posits that reality is a many-branched tree consisting of every possible quantum outcome rather than being a single unfolding history. This interpretation leads directly to Level III multiverse ideas.\\n\\nHistorically, the most popular interpretation, 【25†the Copenhagen interpretation†en.wikipedia.org】, argued that the measurement forced the superposition of states defined by the wave function to “collapse” to the specified value that was measured. In other words, the act of measuring took the indeterminate state and forced it into a determined state. The quantum “weirdness” arises because the value of the determined state can only be calculated in terms of probabilities, and the probability of any given result is usually less than one.\\n\\nIt remains to be seen which quantum interpretation will prevail. However, 【26†the evidence to date】 argues that a multiverse strengthens rather than threatens the Christian faith.\\n\\nIf you would like to see a question about the multiverse addressed in this forum, send it to 【27†[email protected]】.\\n\\n【28†Astronomy】 \\n\\n【29† †www.facebook.com】 【30† †twitter.com】 【31† †www.instagram.com】 【32† †www.youtube.com】 【33† †www.tiktok.com】 \\n\\n  【34† 】 \\n\\n#####  【34† Direct Proof of Dark Matter 】 \\n\\nNASA’s X-ray telescope Chandra has provided a definitive verdict—dark matter exists—that buttresses RTB’s creation model. Search \n\n  * 【1†Explore】 \n  *   * 【19†Publication】 \n  *   * Multiverse Musings: The Double Slit Experiment\n\n[Image 2: Default publications post thumbnail][Image 3: Default publications post thumbnail]\n\n【19†Publication】 \n\n# Multiverse Musings: The Double Slit Experiment \n\n【20† 】 \n\nby  【20†Jeff Zweerink】 \n\nJune 16, 2010\n\nHere’s a multiverse question I received the other day.\n\n> I would like to know how parallel universe advocates use the double slit experiment of firing photons through the slits results in validation of a parallel universe model. Could you explain and elaborate?\n\nAnswering the question requires a working knowledge of the double slit experiment. Rather than reinvent the wheel, the following video explains the relevant aspects of the experiment well.\n\nThe pertinent points are:\n\n1. As an electron propagates from the gun to the slit, it exists in a superposition of different states (going through one slit, the other slit, neither slit, or both slits). The interference pattern on the wall confirms this indeterminate state.  \n2. Researchers measure which slit the electron passed through, which then causes the electron to exist in a definite state (passing through either one slit or the other, but not both).\n\nMathematically, physicists describe the initial superposition of states with a 【21†wave function†hyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu】. Yet any specific measurement of the system results in a definite state. Furthermore, mathematical operations on the wave function correctly determine the probability of each of the possible outcomes.\n\nThe slit experiment’s results are well established—the question is how to【22†interpret those results†en.wikipedia.org】. An increasingly popular interpretation of the experiment results argues that measurements cause an irreversible interaction with the system that leads to the definite result. Rather than the wave function collapsing, it “decoheres” like a single strand of hair disentangling from a large knot. And this is where the experiment becomes relevant to the multiverse discussion. 【23†Physicist Hugh Everett†en.wikipedia.org】 used this quantum decoherence to develop the 【24†many-worlds interpretation†en.wikipedia.org】 of quantum mechanics. This interpretation posits that reality is a many-branched tree consisting of every possible quantum outcome rather than being a single unfolding history. This interpretation leads directly to Level III multiverse ideas.\n\nHistorically, the most popular interpretation, 【25†the Copenhagen interpretation†en.wikipedia.org】, argued that the measurement forced the superposition of states defined by the wave function to “collapse” to the specified value that was measured. In other words, the act of measuring took the indeterminate state and forced it into a determined state. The quantum “weirdness” arises because the value of the determined state can only be calculated in terms of probabilities, and the probability of any given result is usually less than one.\n\nIt remains to be seen which quantum interpretation will prevail. However, 【26†the evidence to date】 argues that a multiverse strengthens rather than threatens the Christian faith.\n\nIf you would like to see a question about the multiverse addressed in this forum, send it to 【27†[email protected]】.\n\n【28†Astronomy】 \n\n【29† †www.facebook.com】 【30† †twitter.com】 【31† †www.instagram.com】 【32† †www.youtube.com】 【33† †www.tiktok.com】 \n\n  【34† 】 \n\n#####  【34† Direct Proof of Dark Matter 】 \n\nNASA’s X-ray telescope Chandra has provided a definitive verdict—dark matter exists—that buttresses RTB’s creation model. - Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "1bf72278-8e87-41e5-8b73-f39de54a571a", "parent_id": "13897f09-b9d1-4def-8f13-fda7b3add331", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:19:37.088951+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"finish_details\":{\"type\":\"stop\",\"stop_tokens\":[100260]},\"citations\":[{\"start_ix\":753,\"end_ix\":764,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"invalid_reason\":\"Referenced message 23 in citation 【23†source】 is not a quote or tether browsing display.\"}],\"is_complete\":true,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4\",\"parent_id\":\"13897f09-b9d1-4def-8f13-fda7b3add331\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\",\"content_references\":[{\"matched_text\":\"【23†source】\",\"start_idx\":753,\"end_idx\":764,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"hidden\",\"invalid\":true}]}"}`


An interesting alternative to the double-slit experiment involving electrons is the study of time-domain double-slit interference using XUV synchrotron radiation. In this experiment, a pair of light wave packets interacts with a helium atom, producing two photoelectron wave packets. These packets spread in free space and overlap, leading to an interference pattern observable in energy or momentum distributions. The experiment uses a two-dimensional position-sensitive detector to record photoelectron images, allowing observation of the quantum interference pattern as it builds up. This method provides insights into the wave-like nature of electrons by examining the interference patterns of photoelectrons generated by pairs of light wave packets【23†source】.

This kind of experiment contributes to our understanding of quantum mechanics and the wave-particle duality of electrons, similar to what the double-slit experiment demonstrates. However, it approaches the phenomenon from a different angle, offering a fresh perspective on how electrons behave under varying experimental conditions. The results from such studies continue to shape our understanding of fundamental physics.

- Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "8c4a4959-3d66-4ad0-bff9-d4dffadb432a", "parent_id": "58ba2f3e-d009-4230-9ae8-5c426de7f9ce", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:20:19.169264+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"finish_details\":{\"type\":\"stop\",\"stop_tokens\":[100260]},\"citations\":[{\"start_ix\":334,\"end_ix\":341,\"citation_format_type\":\"tether_og\",\"invalid_reason\":\"Referenced message 23 in citation 【23†출처】 is not a quote or tether browsing display.\"}],\"is_complete\":true,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4\",\"parent_id\":\"58ba2f3e-d009-4230-9ae8-5c426de7f9ce\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\",\"content_references\":[{\"matched_text\":\"【23†출처】\",\"start_idx\":334,\"end_idx\":341,\"alt\":null,\"type\":\"hidden\",\"invalid\":true}]}"}`


이중 슬릿 실험의 대안으로 전자를 이용한 실험 중 하나는 XUV 동기 방사선을 사용한 시간 영역 이중 슬릿 간섭 연구입니다. 이 실험에서는 두 개의 광파 묶음이 헬륨 원자와 상호 작용하여 두 개의 광전자 파동 묶음을 생성합니다. 이 파동 묶음들은 자유 공간에서 퍼져나가 서로 겹치며, 에너지 또는 운동량 분포에서 간섭 무늬를 나타내게 됩니다. 실험은 2차원 위치 감지형 검출기를 사용하여 광전자 이미지를 기록하고, 양자 간섭 무늬가 형성되는 과정을 관찰합니다. 이 방법은 두 개의 광파 묶음에 의해 생성된 광전자의 간섭 무늬를 분석함으로써 전자의 파동-입자 이중성에 대한 새로운 통찰력을 제공합니다【23†출처】.

이러한 종류의 실험은 이중 슬릿 실험에서 보여주는 것과 유사하게 전자의 양자역학 및 파동-입자 이중성에 대한 우리의 이해에 기여합니다. 그러나 이는 현상을 다른 각도에서 접근하여, 다양한 실험 조건 하에서 전자가 어떻게 행동하는지에 대한 새로운 관점을 제공합니다. 이러한 연구 결과들은 기본 물리학에 대한 우리의 이해를 계속 형성하는 데 중요한 역할을 합니다.

행운은 언제 찾아오는 것일까?

### Message 29 — assistant

- Timestamp: 2023-12-27T01:48:43.137052+00:00
- Metadata: `{"source": "rua", "message_id": "d4f09288-5c07-46b0-a5b3-2e762b182fbf", "parent_id": "1d4b827a-cd96-44fe-9004-e52be216adce", "create_time": "2023-12-27T01:48:43.137052+00:00", "update_time": null, "status": "finished_successfully", "metadata_json": "{\"finish_details\":{\"type\":\"stop\",\"stop_tokens\":[100260]},\"is_complete\":true,\"message_type\":null,\"model_slug\":\"gpt-4\",\"voice_mode_message\":true,\"parent_id\":\"1d4b827a-cd96-44fe-9004-e52be216adce\",\"timestamp_\":\"absolute\"}"}`


행운이 언제 찾아오는지는 신비한 질문이에요. 많은 사람들은 행운이 우연, 준비, 그리고 기회의 교차점에서 발생한다고 생각합니다. 어떤 이들은 행운이 준비된 마음과 열린 태도를 가진 사람들에게 더 자주 찾아온다고 믿기도 합니다. 또 다른 관점은, 행운이라는 것이 존재하지 않으며 모든 것은 개인의 노력과 결정에 달려있다고 보는 견해도 있습니다.

심리학적 관점에서 보면, 행운이란 개인의 태도와 인식에 크게 의존합니다. 긍정적이고 기회를 찾는 사람들은 자신의 경험을 행운으로 해석할 가능성이 더 높습니다. 반면에, 부정적이거나 기회를 인식하지 못하는 사람들은 동일한 상황에서도 행운이라고 느끼지 못할 수 있습니다.

최종적으로, 행운은 개인마다 다르게 경험되며, 그것이 언제나 어떻게 찾아올지는 예측하기 어려운 일입니다. 하지만 항상 준비하고 긍정적인 태도를 유지하는 것이 행운을 끌어들일 수 있는 좋은 방법일 것입니다.

Really, do you feel like you’re a lucky person? Not lucky in the sense that you have a good, or even a great, life, but lucky in the sense that things just seem to fall into your lap? \\n\\nWhether you are lucky or not, you probably think that luck is just random, that people stumble into good things by accident. You may have heard that luck is “preparation meeting opportunity,” but how then do you explain a mere acquaintance calling you with free tickets to a show you’ve been wanting to see, but couldn’t afford? \\n\\nWhat if luck isn’t random after all? What if there is actually a structure underlying luck? Ten years of research by Professor Richard Wiseman of the University of Hertforshire, England suggests exactly this. His research shows that luck is largely composed of four capacities:\\n\\n1) Creating and noticing chance opportunities. You have to be out in the world, and you have to be relaxed enough to actually notice them. \\n\\n2) Making lucky decisions by paying attention to your 【67†intuition】. There is much more happening around us than we can ever grasp in our conscious minds. But beneath our conscious awareness, we are picking up all sorts of cues and clues to what is happening, which we call 【67†intuition】.\\n\\n3) Creating self-fulfilling prophecies by having positive expectations. When you expect things to go well, you will unconsciously pick up the cues that match what you expect – you’ll see it when you believe it. \\n\\n4) Adopting a resilient 【68†attitude】 that transforms bad luck into good. Lemons can be made into lemonade. You can learn from the worst disaster. If you’re always looking for the silver lining in the dark cloud, which is often a life lesson to apply going forward, your luck will improve continuously. \\n\\nOther research of Professor Wiseman suggests that luckier people tend to have wider social networks than less lucky people. This is a kind of virtuous cycle. When you look at the first capacity above, you’ll notice that you have to be out in the world to be in the right place at the right time. Thus, if you want to be lucky, it’s a good idea to cultivate wide enough social networks that you’re frequently out of your cave and in the lucky zone. And how can your social network do anything but expand when you have positive expectations and a resilient 【68†attitude】? Those vibes are bound to draw people to you.\\n\\nSo how can 【69†meditation】 improve your luck? 【69†Meditation】 helps cultivate the capacities for luck.\\n\\n1) Meditation helps you relax. Numerous studies show that meditation increases relaxation, as measured by lowered heart rate and decreased blood pressure, even when the person is not meditating. There is also evidence that meditators recover more quickly from stress than nonmeditators. When you’re relaxed, you notice more of those chance opportunities. (These studies were reviewed by Michael Murphy and Stephen Donovan and are available online at 【70†www.noetic.org/research/medbiblio/index.htm†www.noetic.org】.)\\n\\n2) 【69†Meditation】 increases visual sensitivity, particularly improving sensitivity to visual stimuli before the meditator is consciously aware of the stimulus. Again, seeing before you know you’re seeing helps you notice those chance opportunities.\\n\\n3) Meditation improves empathy, which is the ability to sense what others are feeling. This a big part of 【67†intuition】 – knowing what’s going on for someone else, so you know whom to approach, or when is the right moment to ask for a favor. It’s also a big part of having a large social network, which is related to luck. \\n\\n4) 【69†Meditation】 improves visualization. The more you visualize something positive, the more likely it is to happen, or at least, the more likely it is that you’ll notice if it’s happening. \\n\\n5) Meditation improves psychological health. In different studies, meditators have been shown to be less nervous, less anxious, less aggressive, less depressed, less irritable, less domineering, less inhibited, more sociable, more self-confident, more positive about themselves, more emotionally stable, more self-reliant and more field independent than non-meditators. Being healthy psychologically helps you have positive expectations and deal effectively with life’s setbacks – the resilient 【68†attitude】 that turns bad luck into good. \\n\\nIt’s often been said that “The harder I work, the luckier I get.” But sitting in the “not doing” of 【69†meditation】 may make you even luckier!\\n\\nAuthor's Bio: \\n\\nHollis Polk is a personal coach (【71†http://www.888-4-hollis.com†www.888-4-hollis.com】), who has been helping people create lives they love for 15 years, using neurolinguistic & hypnotherapy techniques, decision science, clairvoyance & the common sense learned in 20+ years of business. She is an NLP Master Practitioner, hypnotherapist & has a BSE in engineering from Princeton & a Harvard MBA. One is when events are truly random, like the lottery. A huge study by Richard Wiseman (more about him later) on both lucky and unlucky people found they had exactly the same chance of winning the lottery. So don’t bother trusting 【61†Mystic Meg†en.wikipedia.org】 for your numbers.\\n\\nBut for everything else, like finding your dream job, meeting your ideal friend or partner, or just having a lucky day, you can massively boost your chances. This research was also primarily done by Professor Richard Wiseman and his team at the University of Bristol.\\n\\nUsing the findings from that 【62†research†www.amazon.com】, here are some tips for you.\\n\\nAnd if you want more help, look out for my brand new 10 day video course called ‘The Power of Luck’.\\n\\n## Top 10 Tips to Get Luckier \\n\\n### 1. Use mindfulness to boost your intuition \\n\\nAfter research over 10 years trying to find the secrets of luck, researchers decided they’d ask lucky people if they used their intuition. They said yes, big time. In fact lucky people use their gut feelings and intuition far more often than unlucky people.\\n\\nWhen unlucky people don’t listen to their intuitive feelings, they find themselves in toxic relationships, frustrating jobs and issues with health. When they begin to trust their gut more, their luck changes.\\n\\nIt turns out many lucky people practice mindfulness, meditation, yoga or some other way of becoming more aware of their intuitive hunches. So if you want to turn your luck around, consider one of our mindfulness courses!  \\n\\n### 2. Try something new \\n\\nHave you heard the saying: \\n\\n> ‘If you keep doing what you’ve always done, you’ll keep getting what you’ve always got’\\n\\nAlthough that quote isn’t true if what you’re doing everyday is saving money(!), it is true for many other things. Doing the same things everyday gets boring. You don’t find new opportunities.\\n\\nLucky people love trying new things. From simple things like going on a different route to work, all the way to trying new jobs, visiting different places on holiday or meeting new people.\\n\\nTry something new today and tell us how that goes! It’s fun, you learn something new and you boost your chances of getting luckier.\\n\\n### 3. Believe you’re a lucky person\\n\\n> ‘No one is luckier than the person who believes in his luck’ - German Proverb \\n\\nLuck is very much about perspective. If you believe you’re a lucky person, you’re more likely to experience luck in your life. \\n\\nIf you believe you’re an unlucky person, you may like to begin by experimenting with the luck journal and writing short luck affirmations everyday. This can help to start shifting your mindset to the lucky position.\\n\\n### 4. Smile more...and uncross hands and legs \\n\\nThose smart researchers did a clever thing to discover this fact. They videotaped lucky and unlucky people and observed how they behaved, without listening to the sound. They noted their body language and then looked at what behaviour matched up with luck. \\n\\nThey found lucky people smiled more and had a more open body language. This had the effect of making them more approachable by others. And more conversations with others leads to more luck!  \\n\\n### 5. Turn bad luck into good\\n\\n> “Remember that sometimes not getting what you want is a wonderful stroke of luck.” \\n> \\n> ― Dalai Lama XIV \\n\\nThis is a powerful tip. Everyone experiences bad and good luck in their lives. But unlucky people see bad luck as just bad luck. But lucky people are great at turning bad luck into good!\\n\\nFor example, if you fall down the stairs and fracture your arm, unlucky people would think ‘oh my goodness, I’m so unlucky. This is typical. What will happen next? Bad luck comes in threes’ etc etc.\\n\\nLucky people think: ‘Oh wow, that was so lucky! I just broke my arm. I could have had a much more serious injury. Thank goodness for that!’ \\n\\nSame scenario - different perspective.\\n\\n### 6. Keep a luck journal \\n\\nI love this idea and practice a version of it. \\n\\nGet yourself a journal and write down two things:  \\n\\n  1. ‘I’m a lucky person and today is going to be a lucky day!’\\n\\n  2. Rekindle your old friendships \\n\\nLuck is very much about your connections with others. Experiments and research by social network companies has found we are connected to everyone else on the planet through around 6 connections. In other words, you can reach anyone on the planet through just six friendships - amazing!\\n\\nReach out to an old friend or acquaintance and have a catch up. A simple way to do this is to spend 10 minutes every week calling old friends you haven’t heard from in a while. If they don’t pick up, move onto the next person. Text if you must, but ideally give them a call - I know it’s quite rare these days to call!\\n\\n### 8. Make a luck declaration \\n\\nComplete the following sentence and write it into a luck journal or any piece of paper. Or even into your phone:\\n\\nI commit to spending the next ___ days to making some time and effort everyday to be luckier.\\n\\nSimple!\\n\\nAnother easy one. Everyday write down: ‘I’m a lucky person and today is going to be my lucky day!’\\n\\n### 9. Expect good luck in the future \\n\\nLucky people expect to be luckier. Much luckier. Unlikely to happen. And take steps everyday to make those goals a reality! For example, I’ve set a funding target of a million pounds for our Museum of Happiness.
